HEADLINE: For Bush, an Early Report Card 

 BODY:
To the Editor:
 Re "The Hostage Economy" (column, March 28): 
 I agree with Paul Krugman. If President Bush's tax cut is so good for the country and the economy, why has he been flying around in Air Force One for weeks on end, at taxpayer expense, trying to "sell" it to the American people? Good ideas sell themselves. 
They don't require an expensive and wasteful ad campaign. 
Mr. Bush's time would be better spent in Washington dealing with the California energy crisis. He preaches daily that the American people deserve a break. But what good is a tax cut to California residents if we have to spend it all on our electricity bill?

TONY LEONHARDT
Los Angeles, March 28, 2001

HEADLINE: Senate Acts to Lift $1,000 Limit On Political Gifts by Individuals 

BYLINE:  By ALISON MITCHELL 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 28 

 BODY:
A day after showing support for a ban on the unrestricted, large-dollar donations that have been flowing into the political system, the Senate voted overwhelmingly today to raise the post-Watergate limits on smaller contributions to candidates and political parties.
 The vote, 84 to 16, came after hours of intensive behind-the-scenes negotiations by supporters of Senators John McCain, Republican of Arizona, and Russell D. Feingold, Democrat of Wisconsin. The Senate's action left few remaining hurdles to the two senators' push for a comprehensive overhaul of the campaign finance law. 
 Senator Trent Lott of Mississippi, the majority leader, said this evening that he wanted a final vote on the campaign law on Thursday night.
A compromise was reached on how much to raise the contribution limits and it averted a split in the coalition of Democrats and Republicans supporting change. All 16 Senators who voted against the increase in contribution limits were Democrats. But many of them were expected to vote nonetheless for the overall McCain-Feingold bill. 
And Senator Tom Daschle of South Dakota, the minority leader, put his support behind the increase in limits, saying, "We are now within reach of a bill that can pass the Senate." 
The sponsors of the contribution increase, Senators Fred Thompson, Republican of Tennessee, and Dianne Feinstein, Democrat of California, said they thought they had increased support for the overall bill. President Bush himself had called for an increase in contribution limits.
"Everybody's rolling the dice one way or another," Mr. Thompson said. "My hope is and my opinion is that it probably will benefit the bill." 
Ms. Feinstein said, "There is a basis now where both sides can come together and back this bill."
Under the amendment approved by the Senate, individuals would be able to give up to $2,000 per election to a federal candidate and a cumulative total of $37,500 a year to all federal candidates and political parties. Those figures would be adjusted for inflation by the Federal Election Commission every election cycle. The current limits are $1,000 per candidate per election and $25,000 overall per calendar year.
Political action committees would continue to be held to a $5,000 per candidate contribution limit per election. And the contribution limit to national party committees would raise slightly, to $25,000 per year from $20,000 per year. 
The biggest battle remaining now, after 10 days of debate, is over the question of whether the McCain-Feingold bill will include an all-or-nothing provision that would result in the entire measure being thrown out if the Supreme Court rules against any of its individual provisions.
President Bush and many other Republicans say such language is necessary to ensure that any legislation is balanced and does not have unintended consequences if only parts of it are found constitutional. Mr. McCain and Mr. Feingold call such a measure a "poison pill" designed to overturn the ban on big donations at the heart of their bill, since most of the constitutional questions concern other provisions, such as the bill's effort to rein in issue advertising. Many senators say the issue-ad provisions violate the First Amendment. 
The importance of this fight, expected on Thursday, was underscored when Senator Mitch McConnell, the Kentucky Republican who is the bill's chief foe in the Senate, promised today to challenge the measure's constitutionality in court should it become law. The Southeastern Legal Foundation also announced that it would challenge the bill if it becomes law.
The centerpiece of the McCain-Feingold bill is a ban on the unrestricted donations to political parties that have steadily escalated over the past decade, undercutting the 1974 limits on contributions. Such donations to the national political party committees -- known as soft money -- reached a record of nearly $500 million in the 2000 election. 
But many lawmakers are equally concerned with the $1,000 limit on individual contributions, which they said forces them to dial constantly for dollars in order to raise enough money for the television-era campaign. Such donations are called hard money.
Mr. Thompson argued that the average cost of winning a Senate seat had risen to $4.5 million in 1998 from $600,000 in 1976, and that now was the time to make a change.
"A lot of blood has been spilled on the Senate floor just to get here and get this debate," he said. "It may be another 25 years before we have another debate such as this. Let's come up with some reasonable amount, index it for inflation, so we don't have to go through this again, because in fact we probably won't go through this again."
But Democratic critics were leery of raising the limits too high. "It would be a great tragedy in our view to finally close the door on soft money and then open up the barn door on the other side to a flood of hard money," said Senator Christopher J. Dodd, Democrat of Connecticut. Mr. Dodd ultimately voted for the compromise.
Adding to the concerns of the Democrats was the fact that their party has caught up with the Republicans on high-dollar soft money fund-raising, but is well behind Republicans in bringing in smaller contributions.
In a sign of the difficulty lawmakers were having striking an agreement this morning, the Senate, in essence, backed two competing proposals. By a 54-to-46 vote, it kept alive an amendment proposed by Mr. Thompson that would have raised the current $1,000 per candidate per election donation limit to $2,500 and the aggregate donation limit to $100,000 to candidates and parties across two years.
The Senate also, by an identical 54-to-46 vote, kept alive a competing proposal by Ms. Feinstein. Her amendment would have raised the individual limit to $2,000 and the two-year aggregate limit to $65,000.
The Senate then went into hours of suspended animation so that all sides could try to negotiate a compromise. At midmorning, Mr. Daschle, who has been working alongside Mr. McCain and Mr. Feingold for the past 10 days, insisted he would agree to nothing higher than the figures contained in Ms. Feinstein's proposal. He said her proposal, when all its parts were added up, would allow couples to contribute a total of $130,000 per campaign.
"That $130,000 a couple ought to be enough for anybody in a campaign," Mr. Daschle said. He added that Mr. Thompson's amendment would allow a couple to spend $200,000 a cycle. "That isn't reform," Mr. Daschle said. "That's a mockery of reform proposals."
But he eventually agreed to the compromise, even approving some final provisions on the Senate floor minutes before the proposal was presented to the full Senate. 
"I support this compromise reluctantly because it is necessary to keep us moving forward," he said, adding, "The real victory here is that the reform coalition has passed another critical test."
Should the Senate approve the McCain-Feingold bill, the battle will move to the House, which has twice passed similar legislation by comfortable margins. But with President Bush telling Republicans that they should not count on him to veto a bill, Representative Tom DeLay, the House Majority whip from Texas, has vowed to do everything he can to stop the bill.
The Senate also voted tonight, 52 to 48, to approve an amendment sponsored by Senator Charles E. Schumer, Democrat of New York, designed to give political parties an incentive to observe current limits on spending on behalf of candidates even if the Supreme Court throws out such limits. His amendment would require the parties to observe the limits in order to qualify for discounted television rates for advertisements.

CORRECTION-DATE: March 30, 2001

 CORRECTION:
A picture caption yesterday with an article about Senate approval of a measure to raise limits on political contributions misidentified a senator in some copies. She was Susan Collins of Maine, not Dianne Feinstein of California.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST 

 BODY:
Firm Invests $240 Million For Stake in Nasdaq Market
The Nasdaq stock market, in another step to bring in outside investors, announced a $240 million investment by Hellman & Friedman, a private investment firm in San Francisco.
 The investment comes as part of a commitment by the National Association of Securities Dealers, which owns Nasdaq, to sell its interest in the market. Hellman & Friedman's investment is the first by a firm unaffiliated with Nasdaq, which is seeking investors to finance its efforts to become a more technologically sophisticated global exchange. Page C7. 

Viacom to Keep Blockbuster Unit
Stock in Blockbuster, the home video retailer, rose slightly on news that its parent, Viacom, was so pleased with the unit's performance that it had decided not to spin off its 82 percent stake. Behind Blockbuster's improved performance is the growth of DVD rentals as a percentage of revenue and a rising market share as rival home video stores struggle financially. Shares of Blockbuster rose 80 cents, to $14.10, while Viacom fell $2.04, to $43.91, amid general market weakness. C4.

Study Shows Rate Rise Not Enough
The California controller issued an analysis showing that the nearly 50 percent increase in energy prices agreed on Tuesday would still not cover the prices the state is being forced to pay for power on the wholesale market. A12.

Arguments Made in Copyright Case
The Supreme Court wrestled with an issue of copyright law that carries major implications for a world in which electronic databases are quickly becoming the standard for archiving and researching newspapers and magazines. The question is whether publishers can reproduce and distribute complete issues electronically without infringing the copyrights of freelance writers who contributed articles. C3.

GE Capital to Sell Satellite Unit
GE Capital agreed to sell its satellite unit to Societe Europeenne des Satellites of Luxembourg for $5 billion in cash and stock. C6.

Poland Proceeds With Share Sale
Eight months after the Polish government agreed to sell shares in the former state telephone monopoly to France Telecom, the plan is unraveling. But Poland still needs to sell the shares because it needs the cash to close a hole in this year's budget. So it has turned to a public offering. World Business, Section W.

Pacific Century Posts a Big Loss
Pacific Century CyberWorks reported a gargantuan loss in its first full-year earnings since acquiring Hong Kong's telephone company, Cable and Wireless HKT, last August. After taking nearly $1 billion in charges for merger-related costs and devalued Internet investments, it lost $886 million. "For 2001, our financial objective is very simply debt reduction," Richard Li, the company's founder, said in a presentation to analysts that spoke volumes about how the world has changed for the 34-year-old entrepreneur, who acknowledged last week that he had not graduated from Stanford University, as the company had said in legal filings. World Business, Section W.

Agere Stock Edges Up on First Day
Shares of Agere Systems ended 2 cents above their scaled-back $6 offering price when the cash-short Lucent Technologies spun off its optical components business. C6.

Comair Cancels a Week of Flights
Comair, the commuter carrier of Delta Air Lines, canceled its entire flight schedule through 9 a.m. on April 5 and said it would call off flights on a weekly basis until a contract is reached with its pilots, who are on strike. C4.

The Slow Road to a Free Economy
Only now are the most successful of the former Soviet bloc countries surpassing their meager level of output under Communism. Economists chalk up the slow progress primarily to underdevelopment and inadequate enforcement of commercial laws and lack of encouragement of private start-up companies. Alan B. Krueger: Economic Scene. C2.

HEADLINE: Z3 in South Carolina: Just Visiting 

BYLINE:  New York Times Regional Newspapers 

DATELINE: GREER, S.C. 

 BODY:
SHROUDED for secrecy, a prototype of the next Z3 roadster recently made its way here from Germany to BMW's only American assembly plant.
 Employees of the BMW Manufacturing Corporation said that only those on a short list of factory personnel were allowed to view the car before it was trucked back to an airplane and flown home to Germany. 
Bunny Richardson, coordinator of corporate communications, would not confirm or deny the visit. "I didn't see it," she said. 
 "BMW has not talked, or even teased, about its new models until it's ready to show the customers; it's the same thing we did with the X5," Ms. Richardson said, referring to the company's sport utility vehicle, which is made at the plant along with the current Z3. "We're not prepared to talk about the timing or models of new vehicles."
Ms. Richardson said the plant was doing everything it could to prepare for future models, whatever they might be. While a Z3 replacement is on the list of possibilities, so are new sport utilities. Even before the X5 went on sale a year ago, there were discussions of smaller and larger models.
Susan Jacobs, an auto industry analyst in Woodcliff Lake, N.J., said preparation for a new Z3 was especially important amid an economic slowdown and stiff competition among sporty two-seaters. "The market for roadsters for the next few years may not be that good," she said.

HEADLINE: Irish G.D.P. Seen Rising 7.5% 

BYLINE:  By BRIAN LAVERY 

DATELINE: DUBLIN, March 29 

 BODY:
Ireland, the hottest economy in Europe, will not be quite so torrid this year, but it will still far outrun its trading partners in Europe and North America, according to forecasts from the country's central bank released this week.
 The bank said the economy could readily weather both a possible recession in the United States and Europe's intensifying problems with livestock diseases. 
 Annual growth in gross domestic product will not match the record pace of nearly 11 percent set in 2000, the bank said; 7.5 percent is more likely. A national outbreak of foot-and-mouth disease -- only one case has been detected here so far -- might shave two percentage points off the figure, because it would crimp tourism as well as agriculture. 
The worldwide high-technology slump poses a more serious threat, the bank said, especially to exports, which will grow only 11.5 percent this year, down from 20 percent in 2000.
In an economy expanding so rapidly for so long, price pressures are hard to avoid, and Irish inflation reached 5.6 percent last year, the highest in Europe. The underlying causes may be more serious than that figure shows, according to the assistant director general of the bank, Michael Casey. Traditional measures of inflation, like a consumer price index adjusted to be comparable across Europe, "tend to flatter us a bit," he said. 
Double-digit growth has also left the Irish public with high expectations that may be damaging the economy from within. Wages in Ireland are increasing at four times the European Union average.
"Earnings growth per capita of 10 percent a year has to be seen with some concern," Mr. Casey said. "It may be time for a reality check."
Government employees are tied into a national wage agreement that promises annual salary increases of about 7 percent. But unions have grown increasingly frustrated with the deal as pay in the private sector skyrockets. Labor strife, particularly for transportation companies, has become commonplace.

HEADLINE: Once Again, the City Beckons 

BYLINE:  By Kenneth T. Jackson; Kenneth T. Jackson, a history professor at Columbia, is the author of "Crabgrass Frontier: The Suburbanization of the United States" and editor in chief of "The Encyclopedia of New York City."

 BODY:
Americans don't like cities. Never have. Never will. Nonetheless, we have built big buildings and big places. A century ago, for example, New York and Chicago were already home to most of the planet's skyscrapers and places like Milwaukee, San Francisco and Memphis, which had not even been dots on a map in 1800, could each count hundreds of thousands of residents.
 Yet Americans apparently did not like the cities they had built, for they began to escape them as soon as possible, not for the life of the yeoman farmer, as Thomas Jefferson would have preferred, but for the pleasures of suburban living. The process began about 1825, when the developer Hezekiah Pierrepont sold residential lots in Brooklyn Heights to South Street merchants who preferred to live near their businesses in crowded lower Manhattan. Brooklyn Heights offered less congestion, better views, cheaper real estate and purer air, not to mention a quick commute by ferry across the East River. 
 As the century progressed, New York's suburban edge moved outward. By 1898, more than 100,000 people a day were commuting into Grand Central Terminal and equally large numbers were entering the city from the south, the east and the west.
With minor variations, a similar process occurred around every American city, and in the 20th century suburbia became the quintessential feature of American life. The results are everywhere visible. Bulldozers north of Atlanta, south of Denver, east of Memphis and southwest of Kansas City are at work constantly, flattening the land and clearing the trees to make room for new subdivisions and ever more chain stores. In the 21 million person New York metropolitan area, the fastest growing suburbs are in Pennsylvania, at least two hours by car from the George Washington Bridge. In Los Angeles, demographers consider the urbanized region to stretch over 14,000 square miles, a space more than twice the size of Connecticut.
At the same time, older American cities are decaying and forlorn. Those that have been unable to expand their boundaries in the 20th century have experienced sharp declines in population. Thus, St. Louis, Buffalo, Pittsburgh and Detroit have lost more than a quarter of their populations since 1950. The cities avoiding this fate, like Dallas, Houston, Phoenix and San Diego, have each added dozens, even hundreds, of square miles to their municipal jurisdictions since World War II. But their densities have decreased because their space has expanded faster than their populations.
Will the emptying of American cities continue? Will the great metropolises of our time, like conquered Carthage, be plowed with salt? Many futurists have answered affirmatively, suggesting that a new science of telecommunications would make personal human interaction unnecessary. 
Such predictions will prove to be wrong. The 2000 census found a record 8,008,278 residents in New York City, not including tens of thousands of illegal immigrants who avoid any government enumeration, and not including celebrities, captains of industry and empty nesters who maintain Manhattan apartments but, for tax reasons, avoid making New York their legal residence.
Eight million is a big number with big implications. Gotham is growing faster than New York State and faster even than its suburbs. It is too early to know whether this is a statistical blip. But the 2000 census may mark a major demographic reversal of a suburban pattern that is now almost two centuries old. New York City, after all, is not unique. San Francisco has also increased its population density since 1950, and Chicago and Boston have been doing so since 1990. Even Los Angeles, long the poster child of the suburban trend, has recently become more like Eastern cities, complete with a subway and higher density. Indeed, in the past 20 years, about 40 percent of Los Angeles regional growth has come in older areas that have virtually no "raw" land.
Such inner-city revitalization is being fueled by immigrants, who are flocking to American cities. New York has always been the quintessential immigrant metropolis, a place of dreams and magic, opportunity and hardship. And everywhere we look, newcomers are giving life to neighborhoods that otherwise would be in decline. Twenty years ago, the South Bronx was full of abandoned buildings; today, it is thriving. Similarly, since 1975 the borough of Queens has become the most ethnically diverse place in the world.
The urban renaissance is also fueled by young childless professionals who find Gotham's catalytic mixing of people, its narrow streets and its hectic pace to be a turn-on. After growing up amid spacious lawns and ubiquitous shopping malls, they now prefer an urban, even an urbane, lifestyle and are willing to swap the usual trappings of middle-class success -- a car and a house -- for bustling sidewalks, outdoor restaurants and funky galleries. For them, density is preferable to isolation.
Why did the city become popular in the 1990's? One typical response is the booming American economy. But in the United States, good years for the economy have usually meant good years for suburbanization and bad years for cities. Rather, the single greatest catalyst to inner-city revitalization has been a sharp decline in crime. Across the nation, homicide and automobile theft rates have been plummeting, especially in the biggest and most crowded cities. 
New York City, in fact, is now one of the safest places in America to live, partly because of a low homicide rate, but mostly because of a low motor-vehicle death rate, made possible by densities that encourage walking rather than driving.
Unfortunately, immigrants without options and yuppies without children cannot by themselves revitalize our cities. Only ordinary families with ordinary incomes can do that. If New York -- or Boston or San Francisco or Chicago -- is to return to the glory of its past, then average men and women must choose to remain in the city after they have children. At present, many families move to the suburbs for the presumed superiority of the schools there. Our challenge then is to improve the inner-city schools so that contemporary families will once again know, as immigrants knew a century ago, that the public schools represent an avenue for opportunity and learning. Until we have that, the reconstruction of our cities will be only a sham.
Great cities are necessary for great civilizations. American cities are still where people of different bents and pursuits come together and where new ideas are born. As Charles E. Merriam, a University of Chicago professor, told the United States Conference of Mayors in 1934: "The trouble with Lot's wife was that she looked backward and saw Sodom and Gomorrah. If she had looked forward, she would have seen that heaven is also pictured as a city."

HEADLINE: The Olympics Can Help Reform 

BYLINE:  By Zhang Liang; Zhang Liang is the pseudonym for the compiler of "The Tiananmen Papers."

 BODY:
I belong to a group of reformers in the Chinese Communist Party who are urging the party to acknowledge its mistakes in the bloody crackdown on Beijing demonstrators in 1989. Despite this, we reformers also urge the International Olympic Committee to grant the city of Beijing the right to be host of the Olympics in 2008.
 Some may find our attitude contradictory. Some human rights advocates in the United States have urged the I.O.C. to deny Beijing the Olympics on the grounds of China's poor human rights record and the treatment of religious dissidents and demonstrators. Others warn that Beijing's rulers may exploit the Games to cement their rule, as Adolf Hitler did in 1936. 
 We respectfully disagree. Although we are as concerned as anyone about China's human rights record, we believe that granting the Games will do nothing to damage human rights and will do something to improve them.
The key to our position is that the Games, if awarded to Beijing, would take place in 2008. By that time, the Chinese leadership will have undergone two shifts of personnel. The current group headed by Jiang Zemin is scheduled to leave the stage in 2002-2003. Their successors will undergo a second turnover five years later, in 2007-2008. We are working to make this change as complete as possible.
That is why we have published "The Tiananmen Papers," a record of the party's decision to crack down on pro-democracy demonstrators in June 1989, and why in April an even fuller record of how the leaders reached that decision will be issued in Chinese. Although the book is banned in China, the Chinese edition is sure to find its way home through the Internet and in smuggled copies.
The party today is deeply split. The reformist group to which I belong believes that the publication of "The Tiananmen Papers" and other actions undertaken by us and by others will help end the grip of the current group of autocrats. The granting of the Games would help this process by committing China more firmly to an open door and providing a focal point for further struggles for the rule of law and human rights.
The vast majority of Chinese people clearly support the granting of the Olympics to Beijing. To be sure, their reasons for this are quite different from those of officials. While the government wants to land the Olympics in order to gain prestige to help consolidate its faltering legitimacy, the people's thinking is more straightforward: Why shouldn't the nation with the largest population in the world finally have its chance to hold the world games? A rejection of China's bid for the Games would allow conservative leaders in China to exploit anti-Western sentiments and enhance their legitimacy in a way that would hamper rather than help our efforts.
The policy of isolating China is neither effective nor necessary. Economic freedom is increasing, and party control over people's lives is declining. The power of these trends was demonstrated by the nationwide scope and vast social base of the 1989 people's movement. Politburo Standing Committee minutes of the time show that a three-man majority of that top body was willing to enter into dialogue with autonomous student organizations. Only the constitutionally irregular intervention of Deng Xiaoping and seven elderly colleagues handed victory to the hard-liners and put a temporary halt to China's movement for political change.
Symbols matter. Just as the Tiananmen tragedy must be acknowledged in order to free China to resume its interrupted evolution toward democracy, so, too, the grant of the Olympics would promote China's commitment to human rights and international cooperation.
Although little known to outsiders, the issue of financing the Beijing Olympics has become a matter of sharp controversy among Chinese leaders. Millions of Chinese live in dire poverty, and government budgets at all levels are stretched to the breaking point. As reformers and democrats, we believe in this matter of principle: While supporting the Beijing Olympics, we are opposed to any grant from the national treasury to pay for the municipality's costs in holding them.

HEADLINE: Drug Research Inadequate, White House Panel Finds 

BYLINE:  By FOX BUTTERFIELD 

 BODY:
The quality of data and research on what works to reduce the supply and demand for drugs is so poor that no accurate assessments can be made, a report commissioned by the Clinton White House and released yesterday has concluded.
 The report, by 15 economists, criminologists and psychiatrists assembled by the National Research Council, an arm of the National Academy of Sciences, took no position in the heated debate on whether to give more attention to drug enforcement or drug treatment. 
 But the experts recommended a series of steps to increase government financing for research into drug control policies and for better ways to gather accurate data. 
Otherwise, Charles F. Manski, the chairman of the committee and a professor of economics at Northwestern University, said in an interview, "We are concerned that 10 to 20 years from now we will still be in the same position, still having these same fruitless debates forever."
Professor Manski added, "The bottom line message is, we simply don't know enough to know the effects of current enforcement policy."
The study was financed by the White House Office of National Drug Control Policy and commissioned in 1998. 
In a statement yesterday, the acting director of the Bush White House drug control office, Edward H. Jurith, said, "We will pay close attention to the report's findings, particularly the need for improved data and research in the area of law enforcement." 
But Mr. Jurith added that drug control "is not a perfect science." In fact, he said, "significant progress has been made in drug research in recent years," citing with approval several studies that the committee criticized as flawed. 
The experts found that even what might seem the simplest measures of success in the battle against illegal drugs are unreliable.
For example, it has long been widely accepted that successful programs to reduce the supply of heroin or cocaine from abroad, along with tougher law enforcement on the streets, tend to drive up the price of the drugs.
But the committee found that the data used to measure drug prices, obtained from the Drug Enforcement Administration when it makes arrests, vary enormously from place to place and year to year.
Similarly, the report said, studies of whether drug treatment works and whether it is more cost-effective than enforcement are flawed because they do not use the scientific method of comparing randomized groups -- for example, comparing one group of prison inmates who have participated in a treatment program with another group of inmates who have not. 
The report noted that the $30 billion spent in 1999 by federal, state and local governments to combat illegal drugs was twice the American cost of the Persian Gulf war of 1991.
The report also said that the number of people arrested in drug offenses in 1998, 1.6 million, was three times greater than in 1980, and the number of people incarcerated in state prisons on drug charges in 1998, 289,000, was 12 times the number in 1980.

HEADLINE: A Hawk's Candidacy Gains in Besieged Colombia 

BYLINE:  By JUAN FORERO 

DATELINE: NEIVA, Colombia 

 BODY:
When Alvaro Uribe Velez, a presidential candidate, arrived here in southern Colombia on a recent campaign swing, he found an eager audience.
 A university auditorium was filled, business leaders showered him with praise and the local news media scrambled for interviews. The fact that the election is not until May 2002 hardly seemed to matter. 
 The reason for the enthusiasm is that Mr. Uribe -- 48 years old, a former governor and Oxford scholar, a landowner and an expert horseman -- is proposing a major expansion of security forces to combat rampant crime and loosen the hold of two rebel groups on the countryside. 
If elected, he says, he will double the national police force to 200,000, and increase the number of career soldiers to 60,000 from 40,000. He would also arm a million civilians as reinforcements. 
"What we need is to put a stop -- radically put a stop -- to the kidnappings and the murders," Mr. Uribe told supporters at Neiva's tiny airport. "Here in Colombia we can either make more room for the violent ones, or we can help the people." 
For Neiva -- a city where rebels have for years kidnapped ranchers, extorted money from business leaders, operated roadblocks and exchanged gunfire with soldiers -- Mr. Uribe's hard-line prescription was just what many wanted to hear. 
But his candidacy has alarmed rights groups and some Colombian officials. They say he would promote paramilitary violence and intensify an already brutal conflict. "We see his stance as a sign of the strongman, militarized solution to Colombia, which won't resolve anything but will just prolong the conflict," said Winifred Tate, a Colombia expert at the Washington Office on Latin America, which lobbies on rights issues. 
Mr. Uribe's rising fortunes are also significant because Colombia has in the recent past been a centrist nation, rejecting right-wing and left-wing candidates, especially those who, like Mr. Uribe, run outside the two-party system.
But there is a growing sense here that an authoritative leader is needed and Mr. Uribe is fast gaining support, particularly among the middle class and the wealthy, underscoring the country's shift to the right as a recession continues and President Andres Pastrana's peace efforts with the rebels falter.
"There's a hardening of Colombian public opinion that is playing into a draconian approach," said Bruce Bagley, a Colombia expert at the University of Miami. "What I am hearing is a polarization of the far left and the military right, with Uribe as the candidate emerging from all of this." 
In Neiva, supporters say Mr. Uribe is the one politician who might be able to end Colombia's 37-year-old conflict, most likely through dogged negotiations from a position of strength. Supporters also like his tough talk about ending corruption and slashing government spending.
"We like the politics that he expounds because it's the politics of order, the politics of complying with the law," said Diego Dario, president of the Chamber of Commerce in Neiva.
Many of his staunchest supporters, the wealthy elite and landowners, can relate to an educated, successful man who comes from the countryside and has suffered at the hands of the rebels. Mr. Uribe says his father was killed by guerrillas.
"Alvaro Uribe has felt the weight of violence," said Jorge Visbal of Fedegan, the Colombian cattle ranchers' association, whose members overwhelmingly support Mr. Uribe. "He felt it in his family. He's known it because it has hurt him."
Detractors, however, see a demagogue in sheep's clothing, a politician who is "looking for national office, so he has modified his rhetoric somewhat," as Mr. Bagley put it.
Critics note that when Mr. Uribe was governor of the economically important province of Antioquia, from 1995 to 1997, right-wing paramilitary groups there grew rapidly. Although these groups have killed villagers they suspect of being sympathetic to the rebels, the provincial government did little to stop them. 
Mr. Uribe also promoted and fostered organizations called Convivirs, which were ostensibly to provide intelligence about rebels to security forces, and he said they helped cut crime and violence sharply.
But Human Rights Watch and the United Nations high commissioner for human rights said the groups had also committed homicides and threatened villagers. Mr. Pastrana, who won the presidency in 1998, stripped the groups of their power, and government officials say they no longer legally exist.
Mr. Uribe also staunchly defends two generals who were retired by Mr. Pastrana in 1999 after allegations surfaced that they had ties to these paramilitary units.
Mr. Uribe says the military is unfairly criticized for rights abuses. And he says that had the Convivirs been allowed to continue operating, they would have significantly reduced the rebel presence in Antioquia. 
"What did happen after I left office in my state?" he said. "The government debilitated the support for the armed forces. The government abandoned the community organizations. Kidnappings increased again. Roadblocks appeared again, and homicides went up."
Mr. Uribe, who is trim and bookish and speaks an elegant and purposeful Spanish, said he does not mind being cast as a hard-liner when it comes to rebels. He said that if elected, he would closely monitor the militias he proposes, to prevent them from becoming paramilitary groups.
He also said his government would continue the peace talks started by Mr. Pastrana. But the rebels, he said, would have to agree to a cease-fire and international monitors in the region that they now control with government approval. If they refused, he said, his security forces would start an offensive.
As governor and, before that, a senator, Mr. Uribe was a member of the Liberal Party, one of Colombia's two main parties. With the Liberal Party supporting another candidate for the presidency, however, he formed his own movement, Colombia First.
In polls, Mr. Uribe remains third behind Horacio Serpa, the Liberal candidate, and Noemi Sanin, a Conservative Party politician with extensive government experience. But Mr. Uribe's support is growing. In a Gallup poll in December, 17 percent of those polled supported him, compared with 5 percent in August. Mr. Uribe faces an uphill battle but political experts say he is doing well for someone without national name recognition. With 14 months to go before the election, they say Mr. Uribe could gain support if President Pastrana's peace efforts falter.
"If things go well for Pastrana, Alvaro Uribe's poll numbers will go down," said Eduardo Barajas, a political scientist in Bogota. "If Pastrana's efforts go down, Alvaro Uribe's poll numbers will go up. For him to win, the peace process would have to not advance very much."
Still, Mr. Uribe, who studied conflict resolution at Harvard and Latin America's troubled history at Oxford, understands that for millions of Colombians, security and conflict are not the only concerns. There are concerns about unemployment, the dire prospects for agriculture and the fact that education is woefully underfinanced and inadequate.
So on the campaign trail, he talks about his accomplishments as governor. He points out that he established 103,000 places for schoolchildren in three years. (Only 54,000 had been opened in the previous 10 years.) And he talks about how he hammered away at corruption, cut the state work force to 5,000 from 15,000 and built 650 miles of roads.
Sweeping through a farmers market in Neiva, he stopped to chat with poor farmers and vendors, chomping on fruit as he conversed about the economy and unemployment.
Spotting a small boy in a torn shirt, Mr. Uribe asked him why he was not in school. The boy simply shrugged, embarrassed by the attention. 
Mr. Uribe turned and said: "This is one of my big concerns. Three million children who are outside of the school system. We have to do something."

HEADLINE: Business on Internet Time; 
The Ups Are Fast. The Downs Could Be Even Faster.

BYLINE:  By JOHN SCHWARTZ 

 BODY:
As one of the brightest lights of the Internet revolution, Cisco Systems has long been looked to as the company that not only supplies the equipment that holds the Web together but also understands how information technology makes business work smarter.
 So when Cisco announced earlier this month that orders for its products had unexpectedly plunged and that revenue would decline for the first time in its 11 years as a publicly traded company, it did not just send shudders through the company itself and its own suppliers. It also raised profound questions about the role of the Internet in the current slowdown. 
 The Internet, with its myriad online connections, speeds the transmission of ideas, good and bad, and amplifies their reach. It has allowed business managers to peek into every link of the supply chain that feeds their manufacturing processes, and to change direction with a nimbleness that would have been unimaginable just a few years ago. 
Yet power incites hubris -- a Porsche fairly demands to be driven at breathtaking speed. The result for the economy can be jarring.
"The supply chain looks a lot more like the stock market," said Andrew B. Whinston, director of the Center for Research in Electronic Commerce at the McCombs School of Business at the University of Texas. "It becomes much more volatile."
And that could help to explain why the economic downturn seems to be happening on Internet time.
Since last summer, the economy has gone from a racetrack annual pace of almost 6 percent to barely 1 percent. Business executives in one industry after another have described being stunned by the abruptness of the drop in orders. Caught by surprise, manufacturing companies have watched their inventories soar.
With all the information supposedly at their fingertips, why were executives so out of touch?
There appear to be several related reasons. As in past business cycles, companies caught up in the boom expanded capacity and output to meet expectations of continued strong growth that simply was not sustainable by all of them together. But added on top of that, it seems, was a sense of complacency that the new management-information tools would provide plenty of advance warning of troubles ahead. That may have been asking too much of them.
"It's important to understand that the Internet cannot change what is going on in the marketplace," said Susan L. Bostrom, senior vice president of Cisco's Internet business solutions group. "You can manage those variables that you can control, but what makes business fun," she said with a nervous laugh, "is that there are always variables that you can't control."
Finally, with so many companies farming out actual production to contract manufacturers, some clearly lost touch with the overall market environment.
The Federal Reserve chairman, Alan Greenspan, normally one of the Internet's biggest boosters, has started to warn that speed has its risks -- and that it complicates his job as well.
Many companies "were caught with their pants down," said Mark Zandi, chief economist for Economy .com, a consulting firm based in West Chester, Pa. "Maybe the technology isn't as good as we thought it was in terms of inventory management." On the other hand, he said, "Maybe it's good, but it's only good if you believe it."
Some of the boosters of the new technologies of manufacturing and inventory management doubt that much of a downside exists. "I'm not quite sure of the problem we're creating by having this good information out there," said Steven Y. Gold, a managing director of the consulting giant KPMG.
Yet other experts say it is no surprise that the technologies that speed up business decision-making and operations can be a double-edged sword. Peter G. Neumann, a computer scientist at SRI International who has studied technological risk for decades, sees it as even more complicated than that.
"Many of the swords have more than two edges," he said, "sort of a Swiss Army Knife with blades in upside down, so that you keep cutting yourself on some of the implements whenever you try to take one out."
The new speed of business is also less forgiving than before, says Allen J. Delattre, an expert in connecting electronics manufacturers and suppliers for the consulting company Accenture. He compares the pace of business just five years ago to driving in city traffic at 20 miles an hour.
"If somebody slams on the brakes," he said, "everybody can slow down." But today, "when we're running stock cars at 200 miles an hour, a slight misstep can cause a pileup with great carnage."
When discussing the downturn in recent weeks, Mr. Greenspan has been going out of his way to lay some of the responsibility for the unexpected force of the current swings to the Internet and tools he has long lauded as helping to reshape the economy.
During the headiest days of the 10-year economic expansion, some commentators insisted that the Internet and other information technologies were powering a new economy that would effectively eliminate the boom-and-bust business cycle.
As things began to wobble, that view has given way to a counter-argument that the dot-com bust could drag the rest of the economy down with it, creating what the economist and journalist Michael Mandel calls an "Internet depression."
But what the Fed chairman is suggesting is a middle interpretation, one that acknowledges the overall benefits of Internet-based technologies in business, but which also warns of the risks.
"The faster adjustment process does raise some warning flags," Mr. Greenspan testified last month. Business managers, he said, have access to more information, but everyone often gets similar signals. "As a consequence, firms appear to be acting in far closer alignment with one another than in decades past. The result is not only a faster adjustment, but one that is potentially more synchronized, compressing changes into an even shorter time frame."
With these factors at work, Mr. Greenspan added, the Fed has actually had to work faster and more forcefully to match the new, faster economy. "The Federal Reserve has seen the need to respond more aggressively than had been our wont in earlier decades," he said.
This kind of volatility is showing up on warehouse shelves across the country. And much of it appears to be due to a combination of a widespread bout of "irrational exuberance," with the rise of supposedly turn-on-a-dime businesses that maintain a vast supply chain of other companies that do much of the manufacturing that goes out under the corporate brand.
Cisco is a leader in relying on many less well-known companies to actually manufacture much of the hardware that it sells. Its own plunge in sales has caused havoc for companies like Solectron, the world's largest contract manufacturer of electronics equipment.
Last week, Solectron said that the sharply slowing sales for its customers would force the company, based in Milpitas, Calif., to lower its own profit estimates and to cut 8,000 jobs, about 10 percent of its work force.
Solectron's chief financial officer, Susan S. Wang, said that to a large extent she and other Solectron executives could see the problems coming. After companies had trouble getting the parts they needed in early 2000, those customers ordered far more goods than they could realistically sell, perhaps expecting that even if the market were glutted their own products would beat the competition.
Looking at the orders from each original equipment manufacturer, or O.E.M., and comparing it with those companies' share of the market, Ms. Wang said, Solectron's executives realized: "Gee, if each one of the O.E.M.'s thinks they're going to gain share, we're going to have more than 100 percent share. That doesn't make sense."
Solectron, however, was in a bind. Even though her company tried to warn its customers a year ago that their manufacturing orders were unrealistically optimistic, they did not want to hear bad news.
"If we had said no, they would have told us, 'You're not a good partner for me -- we'll find somebody else,' " she said. "Our only responsible reply was, 'Yes, of course, we'll try.' "
Thus, Ms. Wang said, the effect of a new technology often hinges on the difference between having a powerful tool and the wisdom to use it well. "It's analogous to a child saying, 'I want to learn how to cut down this little tree here.' And you say, 'That's wonderful, I'm going to give you this power saw. It's the best tool.' "
Ultimately, Mr. Greenspan contends, the aspects of new business technology that helped lift productivity and bring about the boom will also help the economy to bounce back more quickly.
That remains to be seen.
Still, even as hopes fade for a V-shaped line on economic charts showing a quick, sharp recovery, supply-chain gurus say that when the recovery finally does begin, the best-networked companies will recover the fastest.
The answer to avoiding a repetition of the current inventory bulge, they insist, is to continue to improve the technologies so that companies can get a better look along their supply chains and eliminate remaining inefficiencies.
That process cannot be complete until manufacturing and delivery come closer to matching the speed at which data zips around the network. But there are inherent limits to how far that can go.
When a manufacturer like Cisco tells its suppliers to stop or slow production, said Jim Sacherman, a vice president at Flextronics, the second-largest contract manufacturer, "it's not that someone didn't get the message to turn it off." The problem is that "those parts had to move around the world for eight weeks, and they're moving."
Ms. Wang of Solectron offers a similar warning. The companies that demand instant response "think we are back-country yokels who are not 'with it.' " But, she added, "in fact, we are the realists who know what is there and what is not there."
In the future, she said she hoped that "our counsel will be better received."
Still, to Ms. Bostrom of Cisco, the silver lining in the current economic clouds is the recognition that in both good times and bad, "the Internet allows the fast to beat the slow."

HEADLINE: U.S. Growth In 4th Quarter Is Cut to 1% 

BYLINE:  Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29 

 BODY:
The economy grew in the fourth quarter at the slowest pace in five and a half years and profits fell for the first time since 1998, revised Commerce Department figures showed today. 
 The gross domestic product expanded at a 1 percent annual pace in the final three months of last year, after a 2.2 percent pace in the third quarter, the report said. 
 A slower buildup in inventories, a decline in auto production and weaker consumer spending led to the slowdown. Business spending on software and equipment fell for the first time in almost 10 years. 
Growth in the first quarter of 2001, now ending, probably slowed to a 0.7 percent rate, according to a preliminary survey of economists. If that forecast turns out to be accurate, growth over the last two quarters will have been the slowest since the 0.1 percent pace in the first half of 1991.
Today's report showed that fourth-quarter growth was slower than the previously estimated 1.1 percent annual pace, mostly because inventories rose less than originally calculated.
The slowing economy has prompted businesses to lay off workers. Still, with unemployment at 4.2 percent, difficulty in finding skilled employees has made some companies reluctant to eliminate too many jobs. That is one reason that first-time claims for unemployment benefits are not rising faster. The Labor Department reported today that first-time claims fell by 20,000 last week, to 362,000, the lowest level in a month. The four-week average for claims, considered a more reliable gauge, fell 3,000, to 374,750.
"This is good news," said Cynthia Latta, an economist at Standard & Poor's DRI in Lexington, Mass. Although jobless claims are higher than they were earlier in the year, she said, "the labor market is still pretty good."
The report on the gross domestic product reinforced the view that automakers and other companies have been accumulating inventories at a slowed pace. Business inventories increased at an annual $55.7 billion in the fourth quarter, down from a $72.5 billion pace in the third quarter. Consumer spending grew at a 2.8 percent pace, the slowest since the second quarter of 1997. Spending on durable goods like cars and home appliances fell.
Inflation was little changed. The personal consumption expenditures price index, a gauge watched by Federal Reserve policy makers, rose at a 1.9 percent pace after a 1.8 percent increase in the third quarter.
For all of 2000, according to the revised figures, the economy grew 5 percent, the strongest since 7.3 percent growth in 1984. That followed growth of 4.2 percent in 1999 and 4.4 percent in both 1998 and 1997.

HEADLINE: Triple-Feature to Hong Kong; 
Longest Flight Takes Extra Care (and 3 Tons of Food)

BYLINE:  By RANDY KENNEDY 

 BODY:
When United Airlines Flight 821 rises above the runway at Kennedy International Airport this Sunday morning, it will begin the longest daily nonstop flight in the world, from one side of the globe to the other, New York to Hong Kong.
 The flight is planned at 8,439 miles, edging out the next longest contender, Continental's flight to Hong Kong from Newark, at a mere 8,437 miles. (In fairness, Continental did beat United to the punch, starting its flights on March 1.) 
 To get a United jet weighing 418,000 pounds (and that's when it's empty) all the way from New York to Hong Kong without its touching the ground anywhere in between entails many unusual requirements. Among the most important are these: 
The flight attendants must wear very, very comfortable shoes.
The jet must be the largest one in commercial service -- a 747-400 model -- and it must fly almost directly over the roof of planet Earth, passing close enough to the North Pole to see the elves.
The plane's gas tanks must be topped off, with more than 57,000 gallons of jet fuel, weighing almost as much as the plane itself, about 389,000 pounds. (When it gets to Hong Kong, it is supposed to have only about 5,000 gallons left.)
Fuel must be tested to determine the exact freezing point of the load for each flight. (A typical freezing point, at which the fuel becomes like wax, is about 44 degrees below zero. Near the North Pole, temperatures can drop to 70 below.) The pilots keep an eye on the fuel temperature using an electronic readout in the cockpit. If the plane is approaching what weather forecasters call domes of supercold air, the pilots can speed up the jet to increase the air friction against the fuselage, which prevents the fuel temperature from dropping as fast.
"We literally go blowing right through those domes," said Capt. Bob Whitman, who will be the pilot on Sunday's flight. Chuck Smith, the co-pilot, added, "We don't hang around and enjoy the weather."
United must determine how many passengers and how much cargo it can probably take on each flight, using computers and complex weather forecast models that tell how heavy the plane can be and still make it. United officials expect the passenger load to be somewhere between 270 and 340. "We are the extreme end of the service capacity of these airplanes," said Eugene Cameron, a long-haul flight expert for United. A good thing about the polar routes, which were only recently opened up for commercial flights, is that there are virtually no headwinds near the pole, and turbulence is rare.
Before leaving, the airline must pack in about 7,000 pounds of food and drinks (or 11,000 pounds if meal carts, silverware, plates and other service equipment is included). It must include 2,500 pounds of other amenities, like blankets, pillows and magazines. And 2,700 pounds of water. (Annie Chin-Corio, a flight attendant who will work on the Hong Kong flights, said the airline would probably not need to pack more alcohol than on other long-haul flights because Asian passengers, who will make up a large portion of those taking the flight, usually drink less than Americans and Europeans.)
As the plane approaches the North Pole, the pilots must switch off the automatic pilot and take the controls. This is an extra precaution because even sophisticated navigation gyros must work harder to pinpoint a plane's location near the pole, as longitude and latitude lines converge and all directions become south. 
The planes will not fly directly over the pole, Mr. Cameron said. If they did, he said, "the gyros for a second would say, 'Whoa, where am I?' " United does not want that to happen. As Captain Whitman puts it, "We don't want the plane flying up its own tail."
The airline must pay Russia about $8,000 per flight -- about the price of one and a half one-way first-class tickets, at $5,608 per ticket -- for crossing over Russian airspace.
On most of United's long-haul flights, two movies are shown in economy class. There will be time enough (15 hours, 40 minutes, give or take) for three movies to be shown on the way to Hong Kong. For the first flight on Sunday, which gets into Hong Kong at 2:30 p.m. Monday, the sleepless can watch "Red Planet" with Val Kilmer, "Vertical Limit" with Chris O'Donnell and "102 Dalmatians," with Glenn Close reprising her role as Cruella De Vil.
The pilots and flight attendants must bunk down for the "night" -- actually, the sun will probably dip behind the horizon for only about 45 minutes, depending on the time of year. There will be four pilots, two of whom will spell Captain Whitman and Captain Smith, the first officer, when they duck into a two-bunk dormitory behind the cockpit to get some sleep. Then, the two relief pilots will sleep -- or become what the crew calls "bunkies" -- when Captain Whitman and Captain Smith take over again.
The 18 flight attendants will take turns sleeping in their own dormitory, above the aft galley, where there are six small bunks. The flight attendants must change sheets for the next sleeper. They can bring pajamas if they want to. 
"I sleep like a baby," said Patricia Suhoza, who will work on the Hong Kong route. "It's very cozy."
En route, the pilots must rely on 11 flight charts, the most that Captain Whitman and Captain Smith have ever used. On flights from New York to Tokyo, for example, they generally use four. In the cockpit of a 747-400 the other day, they unfolded one North Pole map to show the route they would take Sunday. They had to point off the map to show where New York and Hong Kong would be.
Because of contractual rules, the pilots who fly the route will end up working only 12 days a month. They will fly from New York to Hong Kong, then from Hong Kong to Singapore, back to Hong Kong and then back to New York, with rest stops between each flight. They will do this route once again and then not fly again until the next month.
Captain Smith stressed that this only sounded like a comfy life. "Boy, when you get back home, you have to hang upside down in the garage for a couple of days before you're normal again," he said. "You don't operate any heavy machinery."
Some things won't change. Captain Whitman and Captain Smith will still be carrying their plastic coke-bottle-lens gag glasses, the ones that make them look like Jerry Lewis in "The Nutty Professor." For a laugh, they like to pull them out of their jackets, put them on and say, "These are our landing glasses."

HEADLINE: Investors Flee U.S. Stock Funds 

BYLINE:  Reuters 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29 

 BODY:
Investors pulled $3.07 billion out of United States stock mutual funds in February as stocks sank to two-year lows on a stream of disappointing corporate news, a mutual fund trade group said today. 
 It was the first monthly outflow from stock mutual funds since August 1998, when investors, worried by a financial crisis in Russia, pulled a net $12 billion from stock funds, the trade group, the Investment Company Institute, said. 
 The outlook for March was also gloomy. Stock funds were on track for outflows of as much as $20.9 billion in March, based on activity through March 27, according to an estimate from TrimTabs.com, an investment research Web site. 
A spokeswoman for the mutual fund company Fidelity Investments said outflows from stock funds continued in March. 
If the money continued flowing out of stock funds in March, it will be the industry's worst two-month period since 1990. 
Individual investors also poured more cash into money market funds as they searched for havens amid the stock market turmoil. 
The outflow in February was in contrast to a revised inflow in January of $25.06 billion as the markets rebounded and investors gained a brief sense of optimism. 
In February 2000, before the markets started to decline, investors put a record $53.68 billion into stock funds. 
Stock mutual funds have had positive flows in most months since the summer of 1989. The only exceptions were August 1998 and two months in 1990 when Iraq invaded Kuwait. 
Money markets stayed in favor in February this year, taking in a net $54.88 billion, down from $103.02 billion in January, with more than half coming from institutional investors.

HEADLINE: FILM IN REVIEW; 
'Butterfly'

BYLINE:  By LAWRENCE VAN GELDER 

 BODY:
Directed by Doug Wolens
Not rated; 80 minutes
 Is she spiritual kin to Gandhi? Brave sister to Rosa Parks? Or is she simply a rebellious, self-righteous post-adolescent reveling in an outpouring of attention? 
 The 20-something woman in question is Julia Hill, whose Warholian 15 minutes of fame derived from the more than two years she lived in a 6-by-8-foot platform 180 feet high in a Northern California redwood to save the 1,000-year-old tree she called Luna from Pacific Lumber Company loggers.
Like Joan of Arc, she says she hears voices. The tree, she says, speaks to her. Her prayers are answered. Giddy in her rapport with Mother Nature, she is impelled to poetry, and her guitar-plucking supporters celebrate her in folk songs.
Ms. Hill, who acquired the middle name Butterfly, is the subject of Doug Wolens's provocative documentary "Butterfly," which opens today at Cinema Village (22 East 12th Street, Greenwich Village). Ms. Hill, whose stubborn crusade inspired vigils, demonstrations and hostility, is not an unalloyed joy.
Even the environmentalists of Earth First, who have long viewed Pacific Lumber's logging practices with a jaundiced eye, regard her as a mixed blessing, valuable for the world of publicity she attracts yet, as she readily admits, beyond the organization's control.
When not focused on Ms. Hill's conducting cell phone interviews, reciting her poetry ("glorious golden sun" and "milk-white mist" are among its descriptions) or prescribing "love, love, love" as the curative for the world's woes, Mr. Wolens's camera visits her supporters and her father, a former Christian evangelical minister. He also trains it on forestry experts, state officials, Pacific Lumber's spokeswoman and some of Humboldt County's homeowners, loggers (some of them unemployed), police officers and townspeople.
Among them are one woman who says of Ms. Hill and her vigil: "It has nothing to do with reality, of living here and what's going on with the lives of people. To hear her talk, she speaks in cosmos speak."
On Dec. 18, 1999, after more than two years in the tree, Ms. Hill and Pacific Lumber reached an agreement establishing a 200-foot buffer zone around Luna to preserve it.
Mr. Wolens's film sheds no light on how this agreement was reached.
While instructive on environmental concerns about the impact of logging, "Butterfly" does not reward those who seek dispassionate psychological insight into the zealous Ms. Hill or lessons that might be applied to mediating and solving some of the world's other prolonged conflicts. LAWRENCE VAN GELDER

HEADLINE: Europe Central Bank Leaves Rates Unchanged 

BYLINE:  By EDMUND L. ANDREWS 

DATELINE: FRANKFURT, March 29 

 BODY:
After sending out some of the most muddled signals in its two-year existence, the European Central Bank decided not to reduce interest rates today, and that decision promptly sent Europe's faltering currency back to its lowest level in more than three months.
 The decision by the bank, which sets monetary policy for the 12 nations that have adopted the euro as a common currency, runs counter to moves to lower rates by many other central banks, including the Federal Reserve, in the last few months. 
 Despite clear signs of slowing growth in Europe, especially in Germany, the European Central Bank decided to leave rates where they are for at least the next two weeks. 
The decision did not surprise most economists, who had predicted that the bank would not open the monetary spigot just yet.
Uncertainty about growth and about the central bank's policy has undermined the euro's value against the dollar, even though American economic growth has nearly ground to a halt, while Europe is still expanding at a modest rate.
The euro fell 0.5 percent, to 88.2 cents today, its lowest level since mid-December. Though many analysts expected it to climb this year, it has lost 6 percent of its value since New Year's Day.
Contradictory signals from officials of the bank added to the uncertainty.
Otmar Issing, the bank's chief economist, startled many investors late last week when he said Europe's economy had "significantly clouded over" and would probably not achieve the central bank's forecast of 3 percent annual growth. And Jean-Claude Trichet, president of the Bank of France and widely expected to become the European Central Bank's next president, said the central bank was much less worried about inflation than it had been a few months ago.
Together, these remarks hinted at a change in thinking at the bank, whose officials had been insisting that European growth would remain "robust" and was not in need of any extra support from easier money.
But almost as soon as Mr. Issing and Mr. Trichet had spoken, other central bankers began sending out a decidedly more hawkish message. Ernst Welteke, president of the Bundesbank in Germany and a governor of the European Central Bank, said last Friday that it was "not the time to speculate about changes" and warned against making any hasty decisions.
Because the European Central Bank is governed by central bankers from each of the euro countries, cacophony has been a problem before. And the bank's president, Wim Duisenberg, has been criticized for making confusing statements that seemed to cut in different directions at the same time.
But economists say the bank also faces an unusually puzzling environment. The two biggest national economies, the United States and Japan, have both stalled badly, which will inevitably act as a brake on European growth. But many European countries are still expanding at a respectable rate, and inflation is still running above the central bank's self-imposed ceiling of 2 percent a year.
Private economists predict that over all, Europe will grow about 2 percent or a little more this year, a much slower pace than in 2000 but faster than what is expected in the United States.
Economists now predict that the European Central Bank will reduce rates at its next meeting, scheduled for April 13.

HEADLINE: ART REVIEW; 
And When He Was Bad, He Certainly Was Busy

BYLINE:  By ROBERTA SMITH 

 BODY:
"Neil Jenney: The Bad Years, 1969-70," at the Gagosian Gallery uptown, is an outstanding exhibition whose title invites a snappy retort: Both of them? Still, in those two years Mr. Jenney helped put representational painting on a new course and established precedents for the art of the 1970's, 80's and 90's in ways that have yet to be fully recognized.
 This show may change that. It brings together more of his "bad" paintings than ever before, including several that have never been exhibited. It is his first New York gallery show in a dozen years. 
 Its title almost reads as a deflationary dig at art-show pretension, a joke made on purpose. After all, the paintings are jokes that work really well, which means that they have their own kind of depth.
Who is or was Neil Jenney? A precocious, largely self-taught abstract painter, he arrived in New York from Boston in December 1966 and rapidly developed into one of the art world's most independent and reclusive stars, known for playing hardball during his off hours (in a city league) and for refusing gallery representation.
In the late 1960's and 70's Mr. Jenney became a kind of artistic pace car, a little ahead of the pack but also outside the action in a lane of his own. By 1967, when he was just 22, he was making soft Process Art sculptures and appearing in some of the first exhibitions of the related tendencies -- Process, Conceptual and Body Art -- that came to be called Post-Minimalism. These included "Earth Art" at Cornell University, "When Attitude Becomes Form" at the Kunsthaus in Zurich and "Anti-Illusion: Procedures/Materials" at the Whitney Museum of American Art, all in 1969. 
Yet by then Mr. Jenney had moved on, and he revealed where in his first appearance in a Whitney Biennial, also in 1969: to vaguely representational paintings notable for their haphazard execution and deadpan plays on words and images. Buster Keaton could not have been more droll.
"Fish and Pole," Mr. Jenney's contribution to the 1969 Whitney Biennial, is the first painting at Gagosian, followed by no fewer than 20 like-minded works. The canvas shows a large sketchily painted fish and a broken fishing rod lying in a field of slurred green brush strokes that read as grass. Its title is painted like a punch line in plain white letters across the bottom of its plain black frame. Its buzzy closed circuit of language and image, object and painting can still make you laugh, and then think twice. 
The painting's title pulls apart a familiar phrase: not fishing pole but fish and pole. The resulting two objects evoke an age-old conflict between man and nature in which neither completely wins. We glimpse this inner tension and then glide back to the once-over-lightly surface where paint, image, frame and concept all have an equal weight, one that is light and built for speed.
In the subsequent paintings the light and speed become a bit monotonous, a well-executed schtick. But the best works bounce the viewer back and forth between surface and depth. The subdivided cliches of their titles frame the seemingly banal images with hints of dark thoughts and irreconcilable differences: conflicts between world powers, men and women, art and society, humans and nature.
"Them and Us" shows two jets, one with the insignia of the United States, the other with that of the Soviet Union, flying side by side on a surface of drippy blue. "Brushed and Broomed" compares the results of a freshly wielded paintbrush and broom: contrasting a pile of dust with some red brush strokes on the wall, not intended to overjoy whoever is cleaning up. "Here and There," a widely reproduced work, is nothing but a crisp white fence, a worldly descendant of the Minimalist grid, dividing the greensward in half. "Trees and Lumber" shows several rows of a pine forest; the trees on the left are standing, and those on the right are identical to them except that they have been cut down.
Made at a time when most young artists were avoiding either representation or painting, Mr. Jenney embraced both and put them together in a new way. It was immediately identifiable as post-Pop; today it also looks Post-Conceptual, and ahead of its time. In the thick of the Conceptualist moment, he pronounced painting not only viable but also capable of absorbing all the non- or anti-painting strategies around it.
He wasn't alone. Joe Zucker, Chuck Close and Jennifer Bartlett had a similar process-oriented approach, which broke down painted images into their component parts and procedures. Some of Ree Morton's work had a similar objectness. And William Wegman was combining words and image with flat-footed American humor. 
But in doing all these things at once, Mr. Jenney may have been unmatched. In 1978 Marcia Tucker, who had organized the Whitney's "Anti-Illusion" exhibition, built a show called " 'Bad' Painting" around his 1969-70 paintings, along with the work of Joan Brown, William N. Copley and Mr. Wegman, at the fledgling New Museum of Contemporary Art. The same year Richard Marshall included them in "New Image Painting," a largely forgotten trendlet that preceded American Neo-Expressionism. 
By then Mr. Jenney was otherwise engaged. In 1971 he began developing a painstaking hyperrealism that both built on and contradicted his "bad" paintings. The Gagosian show finishes with three of these works as well as some fairly lame canvases silk-screened with aphorisms, which are best taken as oblique artist's statements. "Style Is Technique Plus Symbolism," begins the most pertinent. 
The paintings' titled black frames, now thickened and elaborately customized to the point of seeming bunkerlike, persist. Not surprisingly, nature is a dominant theme. "Atmosphere" (1984-92) and "Morning" (1996) are gorgeous skylike monochromes, modernist additions to Hudson River School painting.
The most interesting work, and a new one, is "North America Divided" (1992-99), which shows the sheared surface of a large tree stump seen through the top of a rickety split-rail gate and a bit of corroded electric fence. In it Mr. Jenney seems to retrieve some of his "bad" painting's surface weirdness and inner tensions. The realism is almost northern European in its perfection, but the forms are all carefully textured, minutely sculptural, oddly American.
In front of this dour yet exquisite painting, the Civil War, manifest destiny and the cost of human progress flicker across the brain. So does the question of how long it will be before Mr. Jenney shows new work again.
"Neil Jenney: The 'Bad' Years, 1969-70" is at the Gagosian Gallery, 980 Madison Avenue, at 76th Street, (212) 744-2313, through April 13.

GRAPHIC: Photo: "Brushed and Broomed" (1969), by Neil Jenney. (Robert McGeever/Gasgosian Gallery)

HEADLINE: Bush and Brazil Chief Have Politics, if Not Trade, to Discuss 

BYLINE:  By LARRY ROHTER 

DATELINE: SAO PAULO, Brazil, March 29 

 BODY:
With Brazil and the United States at odds as always on trade issues but increasingly driven together by growing political instability in South America, President Bush and his Brazilian counterpart, Fernando Henrique Cardoso, will meet for the first time on Friday in Washington. 
 The meeting is clearly more than just a get-acquainted session between the presidents of the Western Hemisphere's most populous nations. It will find "the two major figures in any free trade area of the Americas trying to put their heads together in advance" of a 35-country summit of hemispheric heads of state, said Riordan Roett, director of the Western Hemisphere program at the Johns Hopkins University School of Advanced International Studies. The summit is to be held in Quebec April 20-22. 
 A hemispheric free-trade agreement was proposed by President Clinton in December 1994 but languished when Congress would not grant him the full negotiating authority he wanted. There has been a burst of activity in recent months, including American suggestions that the deadline for an accord be moved up to 2003 from 2005, but in a recent interview Mr. Cardoso made it clear that Brazil, the key to the talks, would not be stampeded.
"The problem is not the date, it's the content of the negotiations," he said. "Let's be clear," he went on: "The big market for the United States is Brazil, and we know that. The big market for us is the United States, and they know that. So we have more problems and themes to negotiate" than smaller countries. 
For a Republican administration with close ties to business groups hungry for new international markets, the stakes are especially high. As noted in a study published last month by the Council on Foreign Relations, "Brazil's economy is more than twice as large as Russia's, almost as large as China's, and twice India's." 
Since Mr. Bush took office, the Brazilian press has been full of concerns that his years as governor of Texas and his lack of experience in foreign affairs would lead him to value relations with Mexico at Brazil's expense. The fact that the United States has had an ambassador here only 13 of the past 32 months has been interpreted as proof that Washington does not regard this nation of 170 million people as serious or important. 
Mr. Cardoso minimized his countrymen's concerns about the United States' southern neighbor, saying "I don't see Mexico as a threat or a rival" in gaining Washington's ear. But his government clearly wants the new team in the White House "to begin paying attention to this monster country," as Kenneth Maxwell, director of the Brazil project at the Council on Foreign Relations, put it.
The policy memorandum written by the Council on Foreign Relations task force urged the Bush administration to move swiftly to establish a "strategic dialogue" with the Cardoso government. 
"Brazil is the fulcrum" of all of Latin America, the study maintained, not just because of its importance in the trade talks but also because "around Brazil, much is coming unglued." 
The document, prepared by a group of academic and business leaders, noted that "both the United States and Brazil face an escalating crisis in Colombia, instability in the Andean region, and an increasingly complicated relationship with Venezuela" and its tempestuous leftist president, Hugo Chavez. In that sense, Washington has begun looking to Brazil as both a moderating force and a potential ally in the region.
But Brazilians were concerned when Secretary of State Colin L. Powell, during his confirmation hearings, suggested that he favored more of a regional effort to contain the Colombian conflict. 
Mr. Cardoso said Brazil supports the Colombian president Andres Pastrana's Plan Colombia, including the American military aid that has been criticized by European powers and some of Colombia's other neighbors. "The Colombian state is in crisis and needs to be reinforced," Mr. Cardoso said.
But he expressed reservations about any deeper American involvement in the Colombian conflict. "It depends on the form the growth would take, and its purpose," he said. "I think it would be a mistake" for the United States to send troops, he said, adding that "I think that the American people should be more worried about that than me."
Mr. Cardoso, a sociologist who has written many books and speaks four languages, developed a close relationship with Mr. Clinton. But he said he does not expect his background as a left-leaning intellectual to impede his getting along with Mr. Bush. 
"He has a certain simplicity that I think is good," Mr. Cardoso said. "He talks easily to people and isn't formal, and that helps create a sensibility that goes beyond ideological experiences."
