SECTION: Section C; Column 2; Business/Financial Desk; Pg. 1

LENGTH: 1262 words

HEADLINE: Harbingers Of Bad Times; 
The Ranks of Job Recruiters Are Thinning Out Rapidly

BYLINE:  By KATIE HAFNER 

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, March 28 

 BODY:
The job-recruiting business has been very good to Herb Deitz. Last summer, Mr. Deitz, who manages the recruitment business for Unitek, a technology consulting firm that works mostly with start-ups, had 35 people reporting to him, with plans to expand. So when business started to slow in December, he ascribed it to the usual end-of-the-year sluggishness.
 But when the holidays passed, Mr. Deitz's business, based in Fremont, Calif., 25 miles south of Oakland, continued to shrink. Today, he is down to 10 recruiters, with no upturn in sight. 
 As the dot-com economy rose over the last two to three years, the number of recruiters swelled by some 400 percent, Mr. Deitz estimates, attracting people from a wide range of other professions. Many turned to recruiting as a path to wealth. In some places, recruiters, who are often seen as harbingers of economic trends, were in even greater demand than the employees they were supposed to be seeking. 
But the recent industry downturn -- the sudden layoffs and the failure of many of the start-ups that recruiters like Mr. Deitz relied on for work -- is thinning their ranks.
"I haven't seen it this difficult in five or six years," said Mr. Deitz, 52, who has been a job recruiter for more than 20 years. Until this year, he said, he averaged 125 assignments a year from companies needing recruiting help.
"This year so far I've got a total of about 20," he said. And in the last three months, Mr. Deitz added, he has received more than 500 resumes from recruiters in search of work.
Many recruiters say it is the rapidity with which business has fallen off that caught them by surprise. "In other years, it was very gradual, a layoff here or there over a three- or six-month period," Mr. Deitz said. "This time it's been right off the cliff."
He said four clients called recently, all on the same day, to say they would no longer be recruiting new employees. "There's not much more you can do when they say that."
The rest of the recruiting industry, outside of high technology, appears to be relatively unscathed. Ben Meador, president of Meador Staffing Services, a diversified recruiting firm based in Houston, which has offices in 14 states, said the office in Austin, Tex., was the only one suffering.
"We had the very best month we've ever had in the month of December," Mr. Meador said. Paul Hawkinson, publisher of the Fordyce Letter, a newsletter for recruiters, based in St. Louis, was also sanguine about fields outside of technology. Mr. Hawkinson said 2,009 new recruiting firms had sprung up in the last six months.
But parts of the country where technology looms large in the local economy are being similarly unkind to recruiters. Kathleen Sullivan, a former recruiter at Dell Computer, in Round Rock, Tex., near Austin, was laid off last month.
"Just last year, recruiters were in more demand than a lot of the technical folks," said Ms. Sullivan, 47, who came to the recruiting field 15 years ago, after working in a hospital as a lab technician.
Recruiters, Ms. Sullivan pointed out, are like canaries in the coal mine, often the first to feel the effects of a shake-out. Ms. Sullivan said that as soon as Dell's stock began to fall last summer, she knew her job was in peril.
"You could see the writing on the wall," Ms. Sullivan said. "If your job is hiring, and if your company isn't hiring, you need to wonder."
Employment recruiting can be lucrative. Contract recruiters can make as much as $150 an hour. Executive search firms are typically paid a percentage of a recruit's annual salary, which can also make for a healthy income.
The San Francisco Bay area appears to have been the worst hit, at least for contract recruiters, who are paid whether or not a placement is made. "There's been a real backlash against contract recruiters," said Mr. Deitz, who is one of them. "An executive search guy doesn't get paid until an employee is placed."
Chris Turner, a 31-year-old recruiter in Bay Point, Calif., used to attend a monthly recruiters' lunch in Silicon Valley. At the lunches, Mr. Turner recalled, dozens of openings for recruiters would be announced. At the last one he attended, in January, "there were maybe three open jobs," he said, "and 200 recruiters looking for work." He stopped going.
In good times, people like Mr. Turner flock to the recruiting profession from other, less-well-paying fields. Mr. Turner, who sold construction equipment before going into recruiting three years ago, was making $75 an hour working as a recruiter at a start-up. But the company lost its second round of financing and Mr. Turner was laid off two days before Christmas.
Not only has the recent turn of events led to the shrinking of Mr. Turner's savings account, but he is planning to leave the recruiting field altogether, and go into sales for a uniform service company, a move he conceded was "definitely a big leap for me."
Mr. Turner said he loved his job as a recruiter -- while it lasted. "It's a rewarding feeling," he said, "finding a match with someone's skill set and having someone see you in the hallway and say, 'Thanks for helping me out.' " 
But veterans in the industry are not terribly unhappy to see their less experienced colleagues exit. "It's the same as with real estate," said Garrett Browning, a longtime recruiter in Sonoma County, Calif. "When the market is up, all kinds of people just jump in, and it doesn't matter if they're good or not, because companies are so desperate to fill positions."
Mr. Browning said the lean times for high-technology recruiters had been helpful in sifting out the bad recruiters. This is an opinion held with particular vehemence by those who have spent years earning their stripes in the business.
"Spring is coming and they can go mow other people's lawns until they find their next calling," Mr. Hawkinson said.
Mr. Hawkinson's uncharitable sentiment is echoed by many recruits themselves, who hold a dim view of those who come calling. Noah Fiedel, a software engineer who at 23 has already held three jobs with Internet start-ups, has little good to say about his experience with recruiters.
"You're fresh meat to them," said Mr. Fiedel, who only recently found a new job after several months of searching. "I used to get calls at 6 a.m. and I'd get e-mail from recruiters saying, 'We'll give you $150,000 if you relocate.' "
Those promises disappeared, but not the early morning phone calls. "Then they'd call me just to check in, and apologize that they didn't have anything," Mr. Fiedel said.
The one interview he did get through a recruiter turned out to be for a position he was completely unsuited for, Mr. Fiedel recalled. "Every job I've landed has been from word of mouth, or a friend's connection, not a recruiter."
Mary Fetherolf, a technology product manager in San Francisco who recently found a job after nearly three months of searching, had a similar opinion. Ms. Fetherolf said she received a call from a recruiter in response to her online resume. "She said, 'I've got this position in Redwood City,' and I said that's too far," said Ms. Fetherolf, who stated her firm position against the hourlong commute to the south.
"She was about to hang up, then she asked me again, 'Are you sure Redwood City is too far?' " Ms. Fetherolf said. "She's desperate, I'm desperate, but we have different parameters."
Desperation is not necessarily a deterrence. Ms. Sullivan said she would like to stay in the recruiting field, even if it meant relocating. "Have I considered going to sell shoes yet?" she said. "No, not yet. I'd have to sell a lot of shoes."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Herb Deitz, who manages the recruitment business for Unitek, a technology consulting firm in Fremont, Calif., had 35 recruiters reporting to him as late as last summer. But now that number has dwindled to 10. (Dan Krauss for The New York Times); Kathleen Sullivan, a former recruiter at Dell Computer in Texas, was laid off last month. Recently, she attended a career fair in Austin. HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS & BONDS; 
Stocks End Gloomy First Quarter as Investors Look to the Next

BYLINE:  By ALEX BERENSON 

 BODY:
One of Wall Street's longest winters ever finally ended yesterday, as investors closed a miserable quarter with a muted rally.
 Three months ago, the mood on Wall Street was guardedly optimistic. After plunging through the fall, the market as a whole seemed reasonably valued, if not exactly cheap. 
 Optimistic strategists like Abby Joseph Cohen of Goldman, Sachs expected the market to rebound solidly in 2001. And when the Federal Reserve unexpectedly cut short-term interest rates on Jan. 3 by one-half percentage point in an effort to stimulate growth, investors bought stocks with abandon, pushing the Nasdaq composite index up 14.2 percent for the day. 
But the rally soon fizzled, even though the Fed cut rates a second time on Jan. 31. By the end of February, stocks were lower than they had been before the Fed's first rate cut. March brought further declines.
"Every strategist on the street, including myself, put together a table of how stocks do 12 months after the first rate cut," said Ed Yardeni, chief investment strategist at Deutsche Banc Alex. Brown. "Then we did it after the second cut. Then we did it after the third. And while we were putting out this happy news, stocks kept going down."
The market's problems reflect the weakness in the economy, which has slowed to a crawl after years of strong growth. Information technology companies, which led the boom, have been hardest hit, with many posting weaker-than-expected earnings and layoffs.
With no end to the slowdown in sight, investors are shedding the technology and telecommunications stocks they wanted so desperately for most of the 1990's.
After falling more than half from its peak last March, the Nasdaq composite index dropped another 25.5 percent this quarter, losing 630.26 points to close at 1,840.26. (Yesterday, the Nasdaq gained 19.69 points, or 1.1 percent.) It was the fourth-worst quarter ever for the Nasdaq. The worst was last fall, when the index fell 32.7 percent.
"This was one of the ugliest quarters we've seen in a long time," said John Morosani, manager of the $240 million ING Barings Furman Selz small-cap value fund. "What you really saw was the unwinding of all the excesses that were put in place in 1998, 1999 and the first months of 2000."
Individual technology stocks, even market leaders like Cisco Systems, have been even harder hit. Cisco shares dropped another 59 percent for the quarter and are now down more than four-fifths from their peak last March, wiping out more than $400 billion in shareholder wealth. Cisco closed yesterday at $15.81, up 56 cents.
Broader market indexes also suffered this winter, although not as badly. The Standard & Poor's 500 index lost 159.95 points, or 12.1 percent, to finish the quarter at 1,160.33. The drop put the index into a bear market for the first time since at least 1987. (Wall Street generally defines a bear market as a prolonged fall of 20 percent or more in a major index; there is some debate whether the October 1987 crash qualifies, since stocks began to rebound almost immediately after their plunge.)
The Dow Jones industrial average, which is less weighted to technology than the other major indexes, showed the most strength. The Dow lost 908.07 points, or 8.4 percent, to close the quarter at 9,878.78, on top of its 6.2 percent loss last year. (The Dow rose 79.72 points, or 0.8 percent, yesterday, while the S.& P. 500 picked up 12.38 points, or 1.1 percent.)
In a surprising exception to the woes of many technology stocks, Microsoft was the Dow's strongest component for the quarter. Microsoft rose 26.1 percent, after losing 63 percent last year, as investors grew more optimistic about the company's chances for resolving its antitrust battle. Other winners included Philip Morris, the tobacco and food giant, which rose 7.8 percent, on top of a 91 percent gain last year.
Despite this quarter's problems, many strategists remain optimistic that the market is overdue for a rebound. The Fed's rate cuts are certain to help the economy eventually, and on a price-earnings basis, the overall market is no longer expensive, the bulls say. Even some longtime bears have begun to change their stance.
"The market looks a lot better under the surface than it does upfront," said Christine A. Callies, the chief American investment strategist for Merrill Lynch. She noted that many stocks actually bottomed in the fall, and that the stocks of companies whose fates are closely tied to economic growth have already begun to rally. Their gains show that the worst of the slowdown may be over and that some investors are anticipating a recovery, Ms. Callies said.
"In the first few months of a Fed policy change, not everybody thinks it will work," Ms. Callies said. "I don't think that the turn the market wants to see is very far away. I think there should be a sustainable rally in the second quarter."
Then again, lots of investors thought that about the first quarter, too. --------------------

Treasury Prices Rise
By Reuters
Treasury bond prices rose yesterday as a report showing manufacturing activity in the Chicago area sank to 19-year lows in March as separate reports showed consumer confidence and spending remaining resilient.
"I think that the Chicago data is going to suggest to a lot of people that the Federal Reserve is going to have to act more aggressively again and doesn't have time to waste," said Kim Rupert, senior economist at Standard & Poor's MMS.
The 10-year Treasury note rose 21/32, to a price of 100 23/32. The note's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, fell to 4.90 percent from 4.99 percent on Thursday.
The price of the 30-year Treasury bond rose 21/32, to 98 30/32. The bond's yield fell to 5.44 percent from 5.49 percent on Thursday.

HEADLINE: Beijing Agents Detain Another Chinese-Born American 

BYLINE:  By ERIK ECKHOLM

DATELINE: BEIJING, March 30 

 BODY:
In the latest in a series of detentions that are causing frictions between the United States and China, Chinese security agents detained a Chinese-born American citizen last month as he crossed the border from Hong Kong, where he works as a business professor and consultant, the American Embassy said today.
 The continued and unexplained detention of Li Shaomin, 44, a naturalized citizen who earned a Ph.D. from Princeton University in 1988, follows several other incidents in which Chinese-born scholars who are either citizens or permanent residents of the United States have been detained or harassed here on hazy security grounds. Some cases have not been publicized because their relatives abroad fear possible repercussions for the person being held. 
 Although American officials have no legal responsibility for permanent residents, so-called green-card holders, they are so concerned by the recent pattern of interrogations and detentions of visiting scholars that they have debated whether to issue a public warning to Chinese-born researchers who live abroad, Western diplomats said this week. This would be highly embarrassing to the Chinese government, and up to now, the embassy has just protested privately to the Foreign Ministry. 
Another scholar, Gao Zhan, a sociologist who is a research fellow at American University in Washington and a permanent American resident, has been held in isolation since Feb. 11, with the Chinese government declaring that she has confessed to espionage, but offering no details or evidence. President Bush and Secretary of State Colin L. Powell have called for her release.
Several other Chinese-born scholars who live in the United States or in other countries have also been subjected to intimidating interrogations or detained while visiting China in the last year, diplomats said.
The incidents come at a sensitive time in China's international relations, with the two detentions occurring in the same month that China was host to inspectors judging Beijing's application to hold the 2008 Olympics, and as Beijing seeks friendly ties with the new Bush administration.
Political experts here said the ill-timed detentions appeared to reflect the power, relative autonomy and conservatism of the Ministry of State Security. Some speculated, too, that security officials were seizing a chance to put a crimp in the activities of the thousands of Chinese-born scholars, especially in social sciences and international relations, who attend graduate school abroad and return to China for research.
Such scholars are far better able to blend into society than outsiders, possibly enabling them to delve more effectively into China's problems, and many also hold relatively liberal political views. 
Chinese-born scholars abroad are starting to fear that, "with the tightening of the security atmosphere in China it becomes potentially dangerous to be involved in what the outside world, and even many within China, think of as normal scholarship," said Andrew Nathan, a political scientist at Columbia University who advises graduate students.
Mr. Li's recent academic work focuses on market competition and China's transition from socialism, but he has also advocated political change. His father, a former Communist Party theorist, was jailed for 10 months in 1989 for supporting the student democracy movement. But Mr. Li's wife said her husband did not belong to any dissident groups and had engaged only in academic work and business consulting. He also was involved with an Internet company in China.
As with Ms. Gao, who was detained with her husband and 5-year-old son as they prepared to board a plane home after a family visit, Mr. Li's detention came out of the blue, relatives say. Ms. Gao's husband, who was sworn in as an American citizen today in Washington, and son, an American citizen by birth, were released after being held for 26 days without access to lawyers or relatives.
Mr. Li had often visited China with no problems since taking up his post in the marketing department of the City University of Hong Kong in 1996, advising Western and Chinese companies and lecturing at universities. Last May he was invited to Beijing to address a Communist Party meeting on the transition to a market economy.
But on the evening of Feb. 25, he was picked up by Chinese State Security officials in Shenzhen, near the Hong Kong border. His wife, Liu Yingli, learned something was wrong only when he failed to return the next day as planned.
"There was no warning at all," said Ms. Liu, who is also a naturalized American citizen and a lecturer in marketing at City University of Hong Kong. "I've used every cell in my brain, and I can't figure it out."
Ms. Liu reported to the Hong Kong police on Feb. 26 that her husband was missing. She was later informed of his detention by American diplomats who said the Chinese authorities, in keeping with a longstanding agreement, had notified the American Embassy that Mr. Li was being held. She has heard nothing directly from the Chinese government.
An embassy spokesman said that a consular official had met with Mr. Li and spoken with his wife, but that under American privacy laws he could not reveal other details of the case. It was not clear whether American officials had been informed of Mr. Li's alleged crimes.
But some Western officials privately said they feared that the Chinese security forces were trying to intimidate scholars, especially those who have remained Chinese citizens and thus lack diplomatic protection, and might wish to stifle the huge and often surprisingly free exchange of information and research between China and the West.
In 1999, Song Yongyi, a librarian and researcher at Dickenson College in Pennsylvania, a Chinese citizen who had a green card and was on the verge of gaining American citizenship, was arrested during what had seemed a routine trip gathering historical materials from the tumultuous period of Mao's Cultural Revolution. That topic is sensitive, but he had made similar trips before without incident. He was released after an outcry from American scholars. 
The case of Hua Di, a nuclear missile expert who fled to the United States in 1989 but then was lured back for a visit in 1998 with false promises of safety and arrested, was more directly linked to security issues. Mr. Hua had written articles on China's military, which he claimed were based on publicly available documents, and has been sentenced to 10 years for disclosing state secrets. 
Ms. Gao, the sociologist, has studied women's and family issues, and took part in two brief study tours to Taiwan. Her colleagues and family say they see no reason for spying charges.
Mr. Li's detention last month appears puzzling, though his family background is sure to be regarded with suspicion by security officials here. His father, Li Honglin, was a prominent liberal theorist in the Communist Party in the 1980's, calling for a loosening of one-party rule. He ran afoul of Deng Xiaoping, the late leader, who advocated greater economic freedom while preserving the Communist dictatorship. In 1987 he was transferred from Beijing to head a research institute in the southern province of Fujian.
In 1989, after he publicly supported the pro-democracy movement that swelled in Tiananmen Square, the father was jailed for 10 months. He subsequently moved to the United States and now lives with his son's family in Hong Kong.
The younger Mr. Li, who was studying in the United States at the time, signed an open letter in support of the democracy movement. In addition to publishing many technical articles in leading economic and business journals he has written on China's broader need for political change. But in recent years, with the government promoting the introduction of market forces into the economy, his economic expertise had seemed to make him a welcome guest in China, his wife said today.

HEADLINE: Effective Nuclear Disarmament 

 BODY:
Among the most cost-effective defense dollars America spends are those that pay for reducing Russia's arsenal of leftover cold-war weapons. The Bush administration began a review of these "threat reduction" programs this week, saying it wanted to make them more efficient. But there are troubling signs that Mr. Bush is planning to reconsider his campaign promise to increase overall funding for these valuable programs and cut them instead. That would be a serious mistake. 
 During the cold war Washington spent trillions of dollars defending against Russian nuclear, biological and chemical weapons. Over the past decade, for a little less than $6 billion, America has financed, among other things, the deactivation of more than 5,000 Soviet-era nuclear warheads, conversion of more than 110 metric tons of bomb-grade uranium into commercial reactor fuel and safe storage of plutonium removed from Russian weapons. It has also helped underwrite new jobs for Russian nuclear scientists who might otherwise sell their talents to Iraq, Iran or Libya. 
 Last year Congress appropriated nearly $900 million for threat reduction programs in Russia and other former Soviet republics. In the presidential campaign, Mr. Bush expressed strong support for these efforts and promised a substantial increase in their funding. Earlier this year a bipartisan task force headed by former Senator Howard Baker called for spending up to $30 billion on them over the next decade. But yesterday The Wall Street Journal reported that the administration's budget makers were instead preparing to impose substantial cuts.
Not all the programs in Russia have been equally effective. Finding commercial projects to keep nuclear scientists employed has been difficult, and efforts to dispose of Russian and American bomb-grade plutonium have been slow in getting started. But one of the programs now in line for big reductions is the highly successful effort to keep track of and secure nuclear material at Russian bomb sites before it is removed and rendered harmless.
The administration should conduct a careful review, identifying those programs that need to be strengthened or have their funding shifted to more effective efforts. But overall spending in this area should be increased, not decreased. It would be a dangerously false economy to slow the dismantling of Russian weapons.

HEADLINE: The Times Will Publish Personal Ads 

BYLINE:  By COREY KILGANNON 

 BODY:
The New York Times Company announced yesterday that for the first time in its history, it would print personal advertising. The ads will appear in certain Sunday sections of The Times.
 Starting April 22, advertisements from readers seeking romance will run in the five regional Sunday news sections: The City section and the weeklies published for readers in Long Island, Connecticut, Westchester and New Jersey. 
 The announcement comes as advertising sales for The Times and many newspapers have declined, mostly because of the slowing economy. The Times's advertising volume for February was down 6.9 percent, compared with February 2000. 
The company recently raised the Times's Sunday newsstand price and the rate for home delivery. 
"It's been a challenging period for advertising, but adding personal ads is something that's been under consideration for a while," said Kathy Park, a company spokeswoman. The newspaper has tested the waters by selling classified greeting ads on holidays, she said.
Douglas M. Arthur, an analyst with Morgan Stanley Dean Witter specializing in the newspaper industry, said of the move, "With pressure on other forms of classified advertising these days, it makes sense that The Times is expanding its definition and including personals." 
The Times has a partnership with Advanced Telecom to operate the phone services for the personals, to screen clients and to take their ad requests. These would then be sent to the paper's advertising acceptability department, which will accept only ads "in line with the integrity of the rest of our classified advertising section," Ms. Park said. "Any language or phrasing that is suggestive or in questionable taste in the opinion of The Times will be declined." 
Other major newspapers, including The Washington Post, have accepted such ads for some time. In the New York region, suburban papers including Newsday on Long Island print them, as do many city publications, including The Village Voice and The New York Observer. 
The Times's regional sections reach 1.2 million readers. The paper's national circulation is 1.1 million daily and 1.7 million on Sundays.

HEADLINE: Energy Crisis Dims Small-Business Hopes 

BYLINE:  By EVELYN NIEVES 

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, March 30 

 BODY:
The lights stay off at Espresso Bravo until well past dusk these days, and in the back of the cafe, where four refrigerators for soft drinks and juice used to stand, there are two. By next month, there will be none.
 "I'll just sell coffee," the owner, Sultan Alkhraisat, said with a shrug. 
 Mr. Alkhraisat, whose customers call him Sam, opened the cafe three years ago on a street in the city's working-class Mission District. When Internet entrepreneurs started moving in about a year ago, he thought Espresso Bravo, the biggest of five cafes he owns or co-owns in San Francisco, would be his best shot at achieving the American Dream. 
But the Internet people left, while his utility bills keep going up. With the electric bills double what they were a year ago, and gas bills more than double what they were just three months ago, Mr. Alkhraisat has had to lay off three employees, shut off six refrigerators and work seven days a week, 16 hours a day. He averages $850 a month in utility bills per cafe, is selling two of them and is seriously considering closing shop altogether.
"I am," he said, "between the ocean and the fire."
So are many small businesses throughout California that depend heavily on electricity and natural gas. After the record increase of up to 46 percent approved by the state's power managers on Tuesday, businesses that rely on a strong supply of electric and natural gas -- bakeries, restaurants, dry cleaners, coin launderies and hair salons, to name a fraction -- are in a kind of shock, as if just told that they have a potentially fatal illness.
The energy managers, the Public Utilities Commission, said the highest increases, based on a tiered rate system to go into effect in May, would go to "electricity hogs." That designation includes small businesses that need to soak up energy to run. 
For many of these businesses, the new rates mean hard choices. They may have to shut down some equipment to conserve energy, risking a loss in sales. They may have to raise their prices, risking a loss of customers. They may have to shut down altogether.
The Public Utilities Commission said that nearly half the customers of the two utility companies that were granted the rate increase would not see an increase in their bills. This figure is disputed by one of the companies, the Pacific Gas and Electric Company, which estimated that 70 percent of customers would see an increase. 
But whether a household's electric bills go up or by how much, interviews with about two dozen small businesses in two cities, San Francisco and Fresno, underscore that the rate increases will affect everyone in California.
Consumers will feel the effects in higher prices for everything from auto repairs to bread to haircuts. Communities will experience the jolt if businesses that offer basic goods and services pack up and go. Cities could see a loss in their tax bases.
For Miguel Echevarria, who runs El Bueno Sabor Taqueria, a taco stand with four tables, utility bills have reached $1,000 a month, while his burritos, the mainstay of his business, remain at under $4 to stay competitive with all the other taquerias in the Mission District. Mr. Echevarria keeps his lights off all day, but has to run the oven and refrigerators, and he is not sure he can handle another increase. 
"It is only temporary, isn't it?" asked Mr. Echevarria, who speaks only Spanish and has not followed the energy crisis in the news.
When told the rate increases were permanent, he grimaced and said, "We'll just have to see if we sink or stay just above the water."
Dave Goodmon, owner of Goodmon's Auto Repair in Fresno, is planning to raise his prices. "I'm going to raise my rates starting Monday morning," Mr. Goodmon said.
His utility bills, he said, will rise to $450 from $275 a month for running compressors and lifts.
After 23 years in business in the same location, Mr. Goodmon does not want to entertain the idea that he may have to leave. "You don't want to hear what I think about the rate increase," he said. "I'm not a very nice person about that."
Rick Petty, who has owned Blackstone Tire and Service in Fresno since 1989, also plans to raise prices. 
"The electricity costs more to run all my machinery, to run all my compressors," Mr. Petty said. "I have to pass it on to the consumer, just like all the other businesses. Some of my vendors right now are already passing on a surcharge to me that they're relating back to the electricity problems." 
For businesses that relied on Internet enterprises, these are doubly hard times. The Sheirling Salon, a hair and skin care salon and spa in what became known as Media Gulch in San Francisco, for all the Internet magazines there, has seen business drop by 15 percent in the last few months, just as utility bills have skyrocketed.
"It affects us a lot," said Jereny Blas, the manager. "We have to use hair dryers. We have to do laundry. We do everything in-house. Our operating expenses are up, so we added two dollars to all of our services." 
The salon turns off all lights at night (for security reasons, it used to leave lights on) and uses candles instead of lights in the retail area during the day.
Still, with bills ever higher and profits down, the salon has filed for Chapter 11.
"We have to really think if we should stay open for business," Mr. Blas said.
Every single business operator interviewed wonders the same.
"I think the store has to close," said Michelle Tong, who has run New Star Cleaners, a tiny wash-and-fold laundry business in Pacific Heights here, for 10 years.
Ms. Tong cannot afford to raise prices by more than 5 cents a pound to stay competitive, but will not make up the increase in her utility bills.
"It's really terrible," she said. "Too expensive for small business."
Mr. Alkhraisat is trying all he can to reduce energy use and still make a profit. At Espresso Bravo on Thursday, he unveiled a salad bar -- nothing to heat, and no extra refrigeration. He plans to hook his espresso machine to a generator so he does not have to rely on the power company. Still, the energy price increases have made him reconsider the dream he brought when he moved here from Jordan eight years ago. 
Now, he said, at age 33, he is thinking of training to become a fireman. He pulled an application from his pocket to show it was no joke.
"Firemen," he said, "make a decent salary, and they don't have to worry about the utility bills."

HEADLINE: 30 Villagers Presumed Dead After Paramilitary Raid in Colombia 

BYLINE:  By JUAN FORERO 

DATELINE: BOGOTA, Colombia, March 30 

 BODY:
A right-wing paramilitary group that terrorizes villagers began what might be its most audacious assault of the year by marching into a small southern town on Saturday and seizing 30 people, the governor of Narino Province said today. The villagers, who were accused of collaborating with leftist guerrillas, are generally believed to be dead.
 Gov. Parmenio Cuellar told a group of reporters here today that the gunmen arrived in Llorente, a town that had seen little violence until the coca trade took hold in recent months. Mr. Cuellar also said security forces did not arrive until Thursday, five days after the 200 to 300 raiders had come and gone. 
 The raid was seen by Mr. Cuellar and human rights officials in Bogota as a byproduct of the extensive American-backed fumigation of coca fields in neighboring Putumayo Province. Officials in Narino have said the aerial spraying, which began in December, had displaced coca growers, as well as leftist rebels and paramilitary gunmen, sending them to the once quiet region around Llorente.
"We've been saying for eight months that Narino was at the epicenter of the growing conflict and that Narino and other regions near Putumayo were in danger of paramilitary or guerrilla incursions," said Jorge Rojas, director of Codhes, a leading human rights group here. "Nevertheless, no one acted."
Details about what occurred in Llorente remained sketchy. A spokesman for the military, Col. Paulino Coronado, confirmed that 30 people had been taken away by members of the United Self-Defense Forces of Colombia, including about 25 men. If all 30 are killed, the raid will be the most violent of dozens of paramilitary massacres this year. In January, paramilitary forces entered a northern town, Chengue, and killed at least 25 people.
Colonel Coronado confirmed that the military did not respond until Thursday. He said the units "could not arrive because they were covering other areas."
The raiders arrived between 4 and 5 a.m. on March 24, rousing people out of bed and herding residents into a small shabby plaza. A man who was in Llorente when the gunmen arrived said in a telephone interview today that he was among the residents led into the plaza at gunpoint. 
The raiders remained for six or seven hours, questioning residents and going through houses, the man said. He added that the gunmen had also forced a handful of residents to single out those who had collaborated with the rebels. 
"They took them away," the man said. "They put a knife and a gun to them and told them to move on. It was terrible. This had never happened to us."
Llorente, in a lowland jungle area not far from the Pacific, has gone from an isolated dirt-poor community to a boomtown in a few months with the influx of former coca farmers from Putumayo, coca laboratory workers and others drawn by a robust economy flush with coca money.
There is no permanent presence of the police or military. The closest unit is a Marine battalion in Tumaco, a 30-minute trip from the seaside. An army battalion is farther away, in Ipiales in the mountains.
Colonel Coronado said military officials remained unclear over who had jurisdiction in Llorente, the Marines or the army.
Mr. Rojas said the failure to protect the town or arrive more rapidly underscored the contentions in a recent United Nations report that harshly criticized human rights problems in Colombia. The report, by the high commissioner for human rights, said warnings about paramilitary attacks against defenseless villagers often went unheeded.
Human rights groups say the paramilitary groups are responsible for most of the massacres in Colombia.

HEADLINE: Clinton Energy-Saving Rules Are Getting a Second Look 

BYLINE:  By MATTHEW L. WALD 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 30 

 BODY:
The Energy Department is reviewing energy efficiency standards, issued in the last weeks of the Clinton administration, that would require new clothes washers, water heaters and central air-conditioners to use less electricity and natural gas. 
 A spokesman for the department, Joseph H. Davis, confirmed that the new standards were under review, as part of an effort ordered by the White House to look at all regulations published in the last 60 days of the Clinton administration. People involved in the review said the standard under closest scrutiny was the one governing air-conditioners; if left standing, it would save the most electricity and reduce pollution from power plants the most, but would also impose the greatest costs. 
 The rule requires that beginning in 2006, new central air-conditioners (and also central heat pumps, which can heat or cool) run on 30 percent less electricity than under current minimum standards of efficiency. In negotiations that led to the rule, the manufacturers had pressed for improvement of only 20 percent. A lawsuit against the new standard has been filed by their trade association, the Air Conditioning and Refrigeration Institute, which calls the extra cost of building more efficient machines a "cruel penalty" on consumers, to whom those costs would be passed on. 
When the rule was written last year, however, its backers called it a bargain for consumers, and they are even more strongly in support now that the price of electricity has been rising.
The new standards for washers, water heaters and air-conditioners were adopted to meet the requirements of a federal law. That legislation was adopted by Congress fully 14 years ago, but, as the Energy Department sat down to work out the details, Congress blocked the regulations in the mid-1990's.
When the air-conditioner rule was finally issued this Jan. 17, Bill Richardson, the departing energy secretary, said the resulting reduction in energy bills and in pollution from power plants would be "one of the biggest environmental achievements of the Clinton administration." 
Now, though, that rule and the others are getting a second look. Staff members at the Energy Department say the agency's acting general counsel has asked them to prepare memorandums addressing whether the benefits of each of the rules might have been overstated or the costs understated, whether they could be considered an "overreach" and whether they pose a potential for litigation. The staff is also looking into whether any procedural errors were made in the rules' adoption. 
Bush administration officials say they can rescind any rule not yet in force.
"It would be irresponsible and wrong," said Mr. Davis, the Energy Department spokesman, "if the administration, newly elected and sworn into office, couldn't review regulations that have yet to take effect."
Some opponents, however, maintain that with the rules' publication in the Federal Register, the procedure would be more complicated. They also point to President Bush's depiction of "an energy crisis" in defense of his decision to oppose limiting power plants' emissions of carbon dioxide, and to his call for more energy drilling and more use of "clean coal" technologies.
Dan W. Reicher, who was assistant secretary for energy efficiency and renewable energy in the Clinton administration, said, "It's stunning that at the very moment the new administration is telling us there's an energy crisis, they would be looking at rolling back standards that will save us more energy than virtually anything else we've done in 20 years."
Mr. Reicher said the economics of the regulations looked even more favorable now, after a period of rising prices for natural gas and electricity, than when the rules were drafted last year.
Efficiency experts say that in California, air-conditioning consumes 28 percent of the electricity used during peak hours in summer, and that a standard that takes the low-efficiency models off the market would gradually reduce the need for new power plants.
But the industry argues that the cost could exceed $5 billion and that the pay-back period for consumers -- the length of time it would take for their cumulative electricity savings to equal the additional cost of a higher-efficiency air-conditioner -- would be 9 to 14 years. 
The politics of the clothes-washer standard is more complicated. The manufacturers' trade group, the Association of Home Appliance Manufacturers, is pleading to keep the rule in place.
"The whole rationale" behind negotiations that led to that rule was that "the members wanted a standard which they could plan towards," said Edward C. Foley Jr., director of the group's major-appliance and supplier division. "Certainty is always better than uncertainty."
Under this rule, the improvements are to come in two stages, in 2004 and in 2007. The improvement in efficiency is put at 35 percent.
As for the water-heater standard, which applies to both electric and gas models, the Gas Appliance Manufacturers Association petitioned the Energy Department earlier this month to revise it. If it is left to stand, water heaters will need more insulation and may require a U-shaped trap to stop hot water from rising up the pipe when no faucet is turned on, keeping the water in the heater until it is needed. Efficiency improvement is put at 5 to 9 percent.

HEADLINE: STEM CELLS YIELD PROMISING RESULTS 

BYLINE:  By NICHOLAS WADE 

 BODY:
The basis of a possible revolution in treating heart attack patients has been laid by three reports of using stem cells from bone marrow to repair heart tissue in animals. 
 In one of the studies, apparently functional heart tissue was regenerated from the injected cells, the first such success in some two decades of effort. In another, the stem cells morphed into new blood vessels that rescued the heart cells around the damaged area from their usual course of overgrowth and death. In the third, stem cells were used to strengthen pig hearts. 
 The three groups of researchers, based at Columbia University, New York Medical College in Valhalla, N.Y., the National Institutes of Health, and Osiris Therapeutics of Baltimore, said they were a year or more away from testing their animal techniques in people. Still, heart disease experts believe the stem cell work is highly promising. 
"The health care industry would be revolutionized if the treatment of heart failure could be moved from organ transplants down to cell transplants," said Dr. Mark Sussman, a heart researcher at the Children's Hospital and Research Foundation in Cincinnati.
The new research opened "some very exciting doors," Dr. Sussman said, but required considerable further work to make sure it was as promising as it seemed.
Dr. Eugene Braunwald of Harvard, who is the author of a leading textbook of cardiology, said the idea of putting stem cells into the heart to grow new heart muscle cells was "a very interesting approach."
If their animal techniques work the same way in people, the researchers say, people suffering a heart attack would be treated by having cells extracted from their bone marrow. The cells would be sorted and amplified, then injected either directly into the heart, or maybe just into the bloodstream, from which they would home in on damaged heart tissue and on the enlarged heart muscle cells that soon grow around it. 
It may even prove possible, though this concept has not yet been tested, to do no more than inject a heart attack patient with a cytokine, a natural protein that stimulates the bone marrow's stem cells to proliferate. The cells would home in on damaged heart tissue, and repair it. Biologists say it is too early to know if the blood-forming stem cells of the bone marrow are also the heart's own stem cells, which researchers have been seeking in vain for years, or if their remarkable ability to repair the heart is just a general property of stem cells.
Stem cells are unspecialized cells that can turn into the mature cells of the body while replenishing their own numbers so as to remain a constant source of new cells. The cells involved in the new reports are called adult stem cells, and differ from the controversial embryonic stem cells that generate the fetus and adult organism. 
The new results all depend on the recent finding that the stem cells of the bone marrow are far more versatile than supposed and can generate other tissues besides the red and white blood cells, their best-known function. It seems that the cells are a kind of universal clay, so responsive to local cues that if placed in the heart they will develop into heart tissue instead of blood cells. 
In one of the new reports, published in this month's Nature Medicine, Dr. Silviu Itescu and colleagues at Columbia University say they isolated a special kind of stem cell from human bone marrow. The cells, which they call angioblasts, are a subset of the blood-forming stem cells that make the red and white blood cells. Angioblasts generate the cells of the fine blood vessels. Although their existence had been inferred, they had not been isolated before, Dr. Itescu said.
After a heart attack, caused by blockage of one of the heart's own arteries, the muscle cells that were deprived of oxygen die off and the cells around them grow four or five times larger to compensate for the damaged tissue. That is why most people survive a heart attack. But the overgrown cells themselves start to die after a time, and are replaced by scar tissue. The heart then starts to fail.
Dr. Itescu said that his team thought the overgrown muscle cells might be dying for lack of an adequate blood supply, a point that could be proved if the cells could be rescued with a new blood supply. This was their rationale for learning how to isolate the angioblast cells.
They then injected human angioblasts into rats in which heart attacks had been induced by tying off a coronary artery. For reasons not yet understood, stem cells are much better tolerated by the immune system than other foreign cells. Injected into the rats' bloodstream, the human angioblasts homed in on the damaged heart tissue. Dr. Itescu said he had identified the signaling chemical emitted by the damaged heart cells, but declined to name it because he has not yet published his description of the work. 
The human angioblasts generated blood vessels within the damaged rat hearts and prevented the overgrown heart muscle cells from dying. The left ventricle's efficiency plunges after a heart attack, but in the treated rats it recovered by 30 percent 15 weeks after treatment, in step with the growth of new blood vessels.
Dr. Itescu said he hoped that clinical trials would begin in about a year, after he had done more rat experiments to define the safety and timing of a treatment protocol. 
In a commentary, Dr. Nadia Rosenthal, a heart researcher at Massachusetts General Hospital, called the Itescu team's success at growing new blood vessels a "remarkable accomplishment."
The second stem cell article, to be published in next week's Nature, was written by Dr. Piero Anversa of the New York Medical College in Valhalla and Dr. Donald Orlic of the National Institutes of Health and their colleagues. The Orlic-Anversa team purified cells from a donor mouse's bone marrow so as to isolate the most primitive stem cells and exclude those that had already taken the first steps toward becoming blood cells. The primitive stem cells were then injected directly into the hearts of other mice that had been given heart attacks by tying off an artery. 
The researchers found that the stem cells generated new heart tissue by maturing into different cells, including heart muscle cells, the smooth muscle cells of artery walls, and the cells that line the blood vessel walls. The mice's left ventricle efficiency was about 40 percent greater than that of untreated mice. This is the first time, Dr. Orlic said, that new heart tissue has been generated from injected cells. He and Dr. Anversa had expected to see just a few new heart muscle cells, and had gone to great trouble to label the donor cells with a fluorescent green protein so as to be able to tell them apart from the recipient's heart cells. 
"We never dreamed the cells could do this," Dr. Orlic said of the new heart tissue. 
Dr. Ronald McKay, an expert on neural stem cells at the National Institutes of Health and a coauthor of the Orlic-Anversa study, was also surprised by the new tissue formation.
"No one has ever seen this kind of thing before -- complete regeneration right across the graft site," Dr. McKay said.
Dr. Orlic and Dr. Anversa believe the new tissue is functional because the mouse hearts improved and the new cells produced a special protein typical of heart cells that have formed a connective network so as to beat in unison. Dr. Sussman said this was good but not conclusive evidence that the stem cells can make working heart tissue.
Still, Dr. Sussman wrote in a commentary in Nature, "The implications are profound: damage to the heart muscle after a heart attack might be reparable with specific bone marrow cells."
In a third experiment, described this month at a meeting at the Cold Spring Harbor Laboratory on Long Island, Dr. Robert Deans of Osiris Therapeutics said he had improved heart function in pigs by injecting stem cells into the region of a heart attack. The pig is large enough that the improvement in its heart function can be measured more precisely than in rodents. 
Dr. Deans used a second kind of stem cell found in the bone narrow, known as a mesenchymal stem cell. The cells usually mature into bone, cartilage and fat cells. Injected into the pigs' hearts, they developed into heart muscle cells. Dr. Deans said he hoped to begin a clinical trial of the technique in a year, probably on patients awaiting heart transplants so that the effect of implanted cells could be precisely measured after the old heart was removed.
Researchers have been trying for years to repair hearts by injecting different kinds of cells into them. Their persistent lack of success, Dr. Sussman said, may make others wary of the new claims at first. But the present options for treating heart failure are not so perfect: drugs to keep the heart limping along for some years and a transplant when it finally fails. 
"So it would be beautiful and also a phenomenal saving in economic terms and for people's quality of life if you could figure out a way to repair the existing heart," Dr. Sussman said. "I think this therapeutic approach, if it becomes viable, gives us an entirely new way of treating heart failure that did not exist before."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST 

 BODY:
Merck to Lower the Price Of Two AIDS Drugs in Brazil
Merck has agreed to cut the price of two AIDS medicines in Brazil, bowing to pressure from that country's government, which threatened to develop generic versions of one of the drugs.
 The government has now turned the pressure on Hoffman-La Roche, which sells nelfinavir, a medicine also known by the brand name Viracept. The government said that it had been negotiating with Hoffman-La Roche on a price reduction, but that the talks had so far been unsuccessful. Page A4. 

Yen Falls Sharply Against the Dollar
Currency traders in Tokyo interpreted remarks by Japan's economy minister, Taro Aso, to mean that the United States was willing to let the yen weaken, and they obliged by pushing its value down 1.9 percent, to 125.52 to the dollar, a level not seen since October 1998. The yen has lost 9 percent against the dollar this year, and 2 percent against the euro. C2.

Toy Chain Buys Time on Financing
Zany Brainy, a toy store chain that has been struggling with an unwieldy acquisition and the difficult retail environment, bought itself more time to right its finances. Toy manufacturers owed money by the retailer agreed to give Zany Brainy until April 16 to find a new line of credit or renegotiate its bank loan. C2.

Anti-Tax Business Is Sued by I.R.S.
After months of ignoring several dozen business owners who boasted that they no longer withhold taxes from paychecks, the Internal Revenue Service has taken its first civil action against one of these companies. It has also sent letters to workers at these companies telling them that they must pay taxes, whether their bosses withhold them or not. C2.

Bush Officials Hurt by Stock Losses
A falling stock market is spelling trouble for a number of wealthy Bush administration officials who are selling their multimillion-dollar investment portfolios -- and taking some losses in the process -- to comply with government ethics rules. A1.

Britain Rebukes Goldman, Sachs
A government report in Britain sharply rebuked Goldman, Sachs and other financial institutions for their involvement with Robert Maxwell, the flamboyant tycoon whose death a decade ago provoked the collapse of a trans-Atlantic media empire that included The Daily News of New York. The report -- almost nine years in the making at a cost of some $14 million -- said Goldman, Sachs bore "substantial responsibility" in the manipulation that occurred in the market. C2.

Suits Filed in Infant Formula Dispute
A dispute over infant formulas resulted in two lawsuits. Mead Johnson, the maker of Enfamil, sued a distributor of generic formula, PBM Products, contending that PBM was making false and misleading statements about Enfamil. Then, PBM sued Mead Johnson, contending that Mead Johnson inaccurately asserted that Enfamil was safer and healthier than generic formula. C14.

Energy Standards Are Under Review
The Energy Department is reviewing energy efficiency standards issued in the last weeks of the Clinton administration that would require new clothes washers, water heaters and central air-conditioners to do their work with less electricity and natural gas, according to administration officials. The review is part of an effort ordered by the White House to look at all regulations published in the last 60 days of the Clinton administration. A11.

GE Capital in $1.4 Billion Deal
GE Capital, the financial arm of General Electric, agreed to buy the Franchise Finance Corporation of America for $1.4 billion in cash, expanding its lending to Burger King, 7-Eleven, Midas Muffler and other franchises. GE Capital will also assume $700 million of debt. C3.

HEADLINE: Tracing Anti-Abortion Network To a Slaying Suspect in France 

BYLINE:  By DAN BARRY 

 BODY:
He was calling himself Clyde Swenson at the time. He was living in Jersey City and working in construction, occasionally flashing hints of his keen intelligence and religious fervor. Most mornings he attended 7 o'clock Mass before lugging an ammunition box filled with carpentry tools to the job site.
 Then late one night in October 1998, in a suburb of Buffalo about 400 miles away, a bullet fired from a high-powered rifle pierced a kitchen window to kill Dr. Barnett A. Slepian, an obstetrician who performed abortions. Soon afterward, the man called Swenson vanished from Jersey City, leaving behind little more in his apartment than some trash from a Taco Bell and a calendar from a funeral home. 
 Federal agents spent the next two and a half years searching the world for him, convinced that he had pulled the trigger. To find him, about all they had to go on were his real name, James C. Kopp, and his ties to the country's loose-knit group of violent anti-abortion activists. 
Several weeks ago, the agents learned that he was in Ireland, living under another alias and working sporadically as a clerk -- for a time at a Dublin hospital where he adorned his desk with religious statuettes. But by the time the Irish authorities pinpointed his whereabouts, he had vanished again.
Then, on Thursday, French police officers descended upon a post office in Dinan, a town in Brittany, to arrest a gaunt, hollow-eyed man in a frayed sport shirt who had just picked up a package from New York that contained $300. The man was Mr. Kopp.
"He did not look good," Christian Guillot, a manager at the post office, said, adding: "He would start out in French, then there would be a word of English, then Spanish. He was nice, but it was pretty hard to help him. We couldn't figure out what he was talking about."
The arrest of Mr. Kopp, 46, was made possible in large part by the communication he kept with those from his past: two anti-abortion activists in Brooklyn who were charged on Thursday with plotting to help Mr. Kopp slip back into the United States. 
One is Dennis John Malvasi, 51, who was convicted in 1987 of bombing an abortion clinic in Manhattan and trying to bomb a second clinic in Queens. The other is Loretta Claire Marra, 37, figuratively shackled to Mr. Kopp today, just as she was literally shackled to him 10 years ago.
In fact, a law enforcement official said yesterday, "We were looking for Loretta Marra shortly after the event in Buffalo."
According to an F.B.I. complaint, Mr. Kopp and Ms. Marra have known each other since at least February 1990, when they were arrested at an anti-abortion protest in Vermont and jailed for two months. 
There were other shared arrests. For example, in November 1991, Mr. Kopp and Ms. Marra shackled themselves to a heavy metal object outside the door of an abortion clinic in Levittown, N.Y. The object was so difficult to crack, Sgt. Gregory Friedrich recalled yesterday, that the police placed the two protesters on a cart, wheeled them into a van and took them to the Emergency Service Bureau office in Garden City, where they finally cut it open with an abrasive saw.
Both protesters were later convicted on misdemeanor charges. And for years afterward, the Nassau County police kept the metallic object as a training tool in case the protesters returned.
The level of Mr. Kopp's involvement in anti-abortion activity through most of the 1990's is unclear, although he is a suspect in three nonfatal ambushes of doctors in Canada and one in a Rochester suburb. At the very least, he was living under an assumed name in Jersey City for several months before disappearing.
Then, in the weeks and months after the shooting death of Dr. Slepian in 1998, federal authorities pieced together a case for the arrest of Mr. Kopp.
Mr. Kopp was linked to the murder scene by fibers found in his Chevrolet Cavalier -- seen in Dr. Slepian's neighborhood days before the shooting and later found at Newark International Airport -- and by a single strand of hair. 
That led investigators to begin exploring the country's network of anti-abortion activists, the law enforcement official said. "And so Loretta's name surfaced."
At first, investigators could not find Ms. Marra, according to the F.B.I. complaint. In fact, just four months after the murder, Mr. Malvasi told a federal grand jury in Buffalo that he did not know Mr. Kopp, but he refused to answer whether he knew Ms. Marra. Finally, in October 1999, F.B.I. agents spotted Ms. Marra with Mr. Malvasi in front of an apartment building in East New York, Brooklyn.
Mr. Kopp's whereabouts during the first 17 months after the Slepian shooting remains unclear; at one point investigators announced that they believed he might be in Mexico. After his capture this week, though, federal investigators said that they believed Mr. Kopp had been living in Ireland at least since March 2000.
John Farrelly, the chief superintendent for the Garda, the police force in Ireland, said that Mr. Kopp might have chosen Ireland -- a country in which abortion is illegal -- because he could blend in among the many immigrants seeking work in the booming Irish economy. He said Mr. Kopp was believed to have lived mostly in Dublin, where he spent time at hostels in the city center and on its outskirts, in Blackrock, with a group of dissident Roman Catholics.
Mr. Kopp is believed to have held several jobs during his time in Ireland, including a short stint this year at the City of Dublin Skin and Cancer Hospital on Hume Street. Laura Ryan, a correspondent for TV-3 in Dublin, said last night that co-workers told her Mr. Kopp walked with a pronounced limp, decorated his desk with religious statuettes and once carried in a chain saw that he told colleagues was for a class he was taking at night.
Last night, the hospital issued a statement saying that the "person we now know to be James Charles Kopp" was a clerk hired to type routine reports.
It said Mr. Kopp had been hired through a "reputable staff recruitment agency." The agency, it said, has said that his references "appeared to be bona fide," although Superintendent Farrelly said those references were fake. 
The hospital said in its statement that Mr. Kopp was dismissed on Feb. 2 after five days of employment. It did not state a reason.
Around that same time, Mr. Malvasi was being honored for his violent work in behalf of the anti-abortion movement. In late January, he traveled to a Comfort Inn in Bowie, Md., to attend the White Rose Banquet. Its founder, the Rev. Michael Bray, said the annual event recognized those who are serving prison time for anti-abortion activity.
Mr. Bray, pastor of the Reformation Lutheran Church in Bowie, said that a feature of the banquet was an auction to raise money for the imprisoned. For example, he said, Paul Hill, convicted for the 1994 killings of a doctor who performed abortions and his escort in Florida, sent along a letter listing the Ten Commandments. Mr. Malvasi's contributions to the charity auction, Mr. Bray said, included the watch he used as a timing device in a bombing attempt in the 1980's.
"You can understand the level of levity here," Mr. Bray said. He added that the items sold for amounts up to $100.
Unbeknownst to Mr. Malvasi, by this point federal officials had received court approval to monitor telephone conversations and e-mail transmissions at the Brooklyn apartment that he shared with Ms. Marra, and were privy to plans that seemed designed to spirit Mr. Kopp back into the United States. 
Then earlier this month, things went wrong -- for everybody. 
Mr. Kopp had stopped responding to messages left for him in a special e-mail account. On March 8, the authorities say, Ms. Marra left a draft message for him with the heading: "Getting Worried." It read: "What's going on, anyhow? Haven't heard from you in ages. Please let me know how you are."
At about the same time, F.B.I. agents were interviewing people at the Dublin hospital, and the Irish police had learned from their contacts that Mr. Kopp was living somewhere in the city center. 
Then, on March 12, Mr. Kopp left Ireland. "He felt the heat in Ireland," said Hardrick Crawford Jr., the F.B.I.'s acting special agent in charge of the Buffalo office. "He felt their warm breath on his neck."
On March 15, The Irish Mirror reported that Mr. Kopp was in Ireland -- a report that the F.B.I. played down. On March 16, the F.B.I. complaint said, Mr. Kopp mailed, from Paris to the apartment in Brooklyn, a package that contained two Irish birth certificates and a blank Irish passport. 
But on March 21, the biggest break of all came: a message from France left in the shared e-mail account, asking that Ms. Marra and Mr. Malvasi send money to a "Jean Aubrigon" at the post office in Dinan, Brittany.
On Thursday, an unfamiliar and disheveled man went to the post office in Dinan. 
"He was looking for money, waiting for money," recalled Mr. Guillot, the post office employee. "But he did not get it."

HEADLINE: Lessons From Seattle 

 BODY:
To the Editor:
 "As Seattle's Economy Slows, Many Like the Change of Pace" (front page, March 29) should serve as a reminder that economic growth does not always make us happier. 
 Open spaces and an unpolluted environment are sometimes more important than an impressive gross domestic product. So, too, are the senses of tradition, community and belonging that rapid development too often has a way of stripping from us. 
Progress has costs, to be sure. But as a country and as individuals, we must be careful not to get so caught up in the pursuit of prosperity that we no longer think about what success really means.

JESSE LAVA
Middletown, Conn., March 29, 2001

HEADLINE: Pataki Names State Official As Port Authority's Chief 

BYLINE:  By RONALD SMOTHERS 

 BODY:
Gov. George E. Pataki yesterday named Neil D. Levin, the superindent of New York State's Insurance Department and a former investment banker, as the executive director of the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey.
 If Mr. Levin is confirmed by the agency's 12-member board at a scheduled meeting on Thursday, he will become the 80-year-old agency's 12th executive director, succeeding Robert E. Boyle, who announced his resignation in December from the $185,000-a-year post. The Port Authority operates the region's three major airports, port facilities, bridge and tunnel crossings and the World Trade Center. 
 Governor Pataki called Mr. Levin, 47, "a leader who knows how to get things done," and cited his experience as a senior executive in both the public and private sector.
Mr. Levin said in a statement that he would "work efficiently and in the most cost-effective manner to build and operate a transportation network so vital to the region." He said he would not comment on agency operations or on any changes he might seek until he was confirmed.
In Mr. Levin, Governor Pataki would get an official who, like Mr. Boyle, has been seen as a loyal and trusted ally and manager rather than an ideologue or political operative.
New York's governor traditionally names the Port Authority's executive director, while New Jersey's governor names the agency's board chairman. The governors have usually cleared their appointments with each other before announcing them.
Mr. Pataki came in for some criticism in 1995 with his first appointee to head the authority, George Marlin, a former Conservative Party official, because he had not coordinated the appointment with the New Jersey governor.
Jayne O'Connor, a spokeswoman for New Jersey's acting governor, Donald T. DiFrancesco, said that Mr. DiFrancesco and Mr. Levin had met within the past week and that Mr. DiFrancesco had expressed his confidence in Mr. Levin in a joint press release with Governor Pataki.
A native of Atlantic Beach, Mr. Levin received an undergraduate degree in economics from Lafayette College in Pennsylvania, an M.B.A. in finance from C. W. Post Center of Long Island University and a law degree from Hofstra University. In 1981, when he was just out of law school, Mr. Levin got a job as Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato's legislative assistant for banking and securities. From 1983 to 1985, Mr. Levin was chief counsel of the committee's subcommittee on securities.
Mr. Levin left government for the private sector in 1985 and went to Goldman Sachs, where he eventually became a vice president in the firm's financial institutions group. He was named the Pataki administration's first banking superintendent in 1995. In 1997, he was named to the insurance post.
State Senator James Seward of New York called Mr. Levin's appointment to the Port Authority post "a natural choice in that he has a very good working relationshiip with the governor."
If approved by the Port Authority board, Mr. Levin would take over an agency that recently emerged from months of disagreement between the New York and New Jersey governors, which blocked action on many projects. Since the resolution of the disagreements last June, the agency has reached accords on new leases for expanded port activities, airport improvement programs, and raising bridge and tunnel tolls and PATH fares.
The announcement that Mr. Boyle would be stepping down was made around nine months after he was named in a civil suit filed by New York's attorney general, Eliot L. Spitzer, who said that Mr. Boyle had illegally profited from real estate transactions involving a suburban hospital of which he was board chairman.

HEADLINE: WORLD BUSINESS BRIEFING: ASIA; 
YEN PLUNGES AGAINST DOLLAR

BYLINE:  Bloomberg News 

 BODY:
 Currency traders in Tokyo interpreted remarks by Japan's economy minister, Taro Aso, to mean that the United States was willing to let the yen weaken, and they obliged by pushing its value down 1.9 percent, to 125.52 to the dollar, a level not seen since October 1998. The yen has lost 9 percent of its value against the dollar this year, and 2 percent against the euro.

HEADLINE: National Briefing 

 BODY:
 WEST

CALIFORNIA: SECOND OFFICER PLEADS GUILTY -- A second Los Angeles policeman has agreed to a plea deal and will help an investigation of abuses at an antigang unit. The officer, Nino Durden, said he and his partner had shot an unarmed, handcuffed gang member. He pleaded guilty to state charges and is expected to receive nearly eight years in prison. James Sterngold (NYT)

ROCKIES

COLORADO: COYOTES ARE SPARED -- Lawmakers killed a plan to shoot coyotes in western Colorado to see if they were responsible for a decline in mule deer. The Division of Wildlife had proposed the shootings because the state's deer population, roughly 545,000, is about 90,000 fewer than desired. Environmentalists had criticized the plan as a concession to hunters. (NYT)

SOUTHWEST

TEXAS: O'HAIR KIDNAPPER SENTENCED -- David Waters, accused of devising a kidnapping scheme that ended in the murder of the atheist Madalyn Murray O'Hair and two family members, was sentenced to 20 years in prison on a federal extortion charge. Mr. Waters, 54, admitted in January doing "physical violence" to Ms. O'Hair, 76, her son Jon Garth Murray, 40, and granddaughter Robin Murray O'Hair, 30. (AP)

MIDWEST

INDIANA: STUDENT KILLED AT SCHOOL -- A 10th-grader was killed in a parking lot outside Lew Wallace High School in Gary by a 17-year-old former student who shot him in the head, the authorities said. The suspect admitted shooting the student, Neal Boyd, 16, the police chief, John Roby, said. The police said they did not know whether the boys knew each other; the motive was unclear. (AP)

INDIANA: PRE-ABORTION COUNSELING LAW STRUCK DOWN -- A federal judge struck down part of a state law requiring women to undergo in-person counseling a day before an abortion. Judge David Hamilton of Federal District Court ruled that not letting women obtain information about alternatives to abortion other than in person unlawfully impeded their right to end a pregnancy. (AP)

ILLINOIS: BILL TO RESTRICT PROSECUTORS GAINS -- The House of Representatives passed a bill raising the prosecutor's burden in criminal cases. It would make it harder to use jailhouse informants and make it easier to set aside convictions if prosecutors fail to share material evidence with defense lawyers. Pam Belluck (NYT)

SOUTH

MISSISSIPPI: GOVERNOR'S VETOES OVERRIDDEN -- Gov. Ronnie Musgrove vetoed about half the state's $3.6 billion general fund budget, warning legislators that their economic projections were too optimistic, but state lawmakers overrode the vetoes. They said the governor, a Democrat, could make cuts if the economy did not pick up. Mr. Musgrove particularly opposed plans to raise spending on privately run prisons, saying the state already had many empty prison beds. (NYT)

TENNESSEE: STUDENT DIED OF HAZING -- Joseph T. Green Jr., a Tennessee State University student who collapsed while jogging on Jan. 29, died as a result of fraternity hazing, a Nashville medical examiner ruled. Mr. Green, of Antioch, Tenn., was being initiated by Omega Psi Phi. He had heart and breathing problems and a temperature of 103.7 degrees before he died. Kevin Sack (NYT)

TENNESSEE: EXECUTION IS STAYED -- The State Supreme Court granted a reprieve to Philip Ray Workman 45 minutes before he was to die by lethal injection. The court voted 3 to 2 to stay the execution and ordered the trial court in Memphis to hold a hearing on whether new ballistics evidence could have led to a different verdict. Mr. Workman, 47, was convicted of killing Lt. Ronald Oliver after a restaurant robbery, but he has said Lieutenant Oliver was killed by another officer. Kevin Sack (NYT)

WASHINGTON

BUSH SWIFTLY FILLING POSTS -- After two months in office, President Bush has filled cabinet and top subcabinet positions at a pace faster than President Bill Clinton, said Paul C. Light of the Brookings Institution, who tracks the process. A total of 484 posts require Senate confirmation; 90 nominees have been announced, 25 others who have been confirmed and 18 have been formally nominated, with their paperwork submitted for review. Clay Johnson, Mr. Bush's personnel director, said the administration had chosen about 270 appointees, but many had not yet been announced. (NYT)

NO COLLUSION FOUND IN GAS PRICE RISE -- Some energy producers withheld supplies of gasoline to maximize profits last summer, but there is no evidence that companies conspired to raise gas prices, the Federal Trade Commission has concluded. The F.T.C. said that a variety of factors, including many beyond the control of producers and marketers, were partly responsible for gasoline prices that soared beyond $2 a gallon in parts of the Midwest. (AP)

SCIENCE AND HEALTH

SOLAR-FLARE DISRUPTIONS EXPECTED -- The biggest solar flares in a decade are expected to disrupt radio communications this weekend in the northern United States and Canada, the National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration says. Clouds of electrified gas may cause blackouts today of ham radio and aircraft navigation channels. Philip J. Hilts (NYT)
