HEADLINE: Westchester's 10-Mile Strip Mall; 
Commerce Crowds One Corridor to Keep Suburbs Tidy

BYLINE:  By JANE GROSS 

DATELINE: GREENBURGH, N.Y., March 30 

 BODY:
The ugliest 10 miles in Westchester County, Central Park Avenue, runs from the Bronx border to White Plains and is lined with indistinguishable strip malls, superstores and fast-food joints.
 This charmless commercial corridor is everything that Westchester County aspires not to be. It is home to the sort of development that would be cursed in Croton, despised in Dobbs Ferry and litigated in Larchmont. 
 So why put up with it?
The answer, planners and residents agree, is that Central Avenue, as it is known, allows Westchester to be Westchester: a lush suburban area with little of the homogeneous sprawl of Long Island or New Jersey. A collection of 39 towns and villages that fiercely defend their idiosyncratic charms. A place where Nimby battles rage over Ikea, Stew Leonard's or other stores like them -- unless they happen to be on Central Avenue. 
"Aesthetically, it's a disaster," said Anthony Cupaiuolo, a professor of public administration at Pace University. "On the other hand, it's very functional. It allows us to have our cake and eat it too."
From its southern terminus, where it melts into the New York State Thruway near the sad husk of Yonkers Raceway, to its end point at the utilitarian County Center, Central Avenue is a place to study the county's evolving retail and culinary tastes, its patterns of immigration and its deep loyalty to institutions that may seem fusty to newcomers but mean the world to those raised here.
"It's a magnet," said Charles M. Feuer, 56, a real estate lawyer who grew up in Larchmont, broke in his driver's license on Central Avenue and still travels from Armonk to shop here. "It draws you back because it brings back the past."
The commercial strip began to take shape in 1956, with the opening of Korvettes in the Midway Shopping Center, one of the oldest malls in the region. The Grand Concourse in the Bronx was fading and an exodus was in the making, both of residents and of retailers seeking greener pastures.
Korvettes drew housewives looking for bargain lingerie and teenagers in search of cheap 45's. Eventually Korvettes gave way to Mays and then to the Burlington Coat Factory, establishing a pattern that survives today, with older chains morphing into the Wiz, Bed Bath & Beyond and Today's Man -- not big changes in the arc of time.
But trends emerge, consistent with the breeding and nesting habits of today's families. These days, stores catering to 30-somethings with young children cannot open fast enough: Zany Brainy, Buy Buy Baby and Babies "R" Us, all within blocks. The same is true of furniture stores. "You can buy leather couches in 42 different styles," said Elliott Sclar, a professor of urban planning at Columbia University who lives in Larchmont.
In cuisine, as in retail, newcomers are often updated variations of their predecessors. Adventurers' Inn, with kiddie rides and hot dogs, was replaced by Nathan's. There were once two diners on a rocky promontory in Yonkers. Only one remains, Mont Olympus, still a comforting cliche, with round-the-clock breakfast and bottomless cups of coffee.
Mont Olympus, and its sister restaurant, Mont Parnasse in Greenburgh, coexist peaceably in the new world of T.G.I. Friday's, Pizza and Brew and even the City Limits, a faux diner that turns cooking into cuisine. But only for the real diners, these two owned by Elias Lurentzatos, do hardy loyalists come from as far as Riverdale or Rockland County. 
Here, literally at ground level, Mr. Lurentzatos sees the first flicker of social change. Once diners were teenage hangouts. But they lost the high school crowd, he said, when smoking was banned, jukeboxes lost their allure and cellular phones replaced pay telephones as a way to summon friends and reassure parents.
Economic upheaval also announces itself in mundane ways. The failing Japanese economy has taken its toll at Meidi-Ya, a convenience store selling everything from seaweed to Japanese-language videos. In the late 1980's, stores like this bustled because of an influx of Japanese businessmen with rich living allowances. Now, the market is quiet, except around a bulletin board where those transferring back to Japan advertise houses and cars, and those staying behind promote yard sales to supplement their incomes.
Since its opening in 1871, when it took a horse and buggy five hours to travel the dirt road end to end, Central Avenue, or Route 100, has been shaped by southern Westchester's particular history and topography. In the metropolitan area's oldest suburb, settlement followed the north-south paths of the Hudson River, the stage-coach lines and the railroad, not the automobile.
With the railroad station as village green and rivers, valleys and ridge lines as natural barriers to sprawl, the county is defined by small, distinctive communities and cumbersome east-west travel. As a result, at least before the opening of the Westchester, a glittering indoor mall, Central Avenue was the sole regional mecca for commerce, attracting people from both sides of the county. 
"We draw pretty equally," said Lyle Steinberg, the second-generation owner of two shopping centers on Central Avenue, just about the only part of Westchester that is permitted -- no, encouraged -- to look like Anywhere U.S.A.
Mr. Steinberg's family owned plenty of land here but strategically located their businesses near the intersection of Ardsley Road. This is the only east-west street for miles around, changing names, doglegging this way and that, but finally connecting the Hudson River side of the county and Long Island Sound.
Both of Mr. Steinberg's shopping centers are in the town of Greenburgh, one of three municipalities, along with Yonkers and White Plains, on Central Avenue. Different jurisdictions mean different sales taxes, different sources of money for road improvements and different attitudes toward controlling helter-skelter development.
In other ways, though, Central Avenue transcends Westchester's ZIP code pecking order.
For example, while you can get status points for living in the part of Yonkers with a Bronxville post office, fancy addresses mean nothing on Central Avenue. The Fine Arts Theater, Westchester's only art house, has a Scarsdale P.O. But Meyer Ackerman, owner of the movie theater that opened in 1972 with Peter O'Toole in "The Ruling Class," is certain it makes no difference. 
The theater draws film buffs who line up outdoors in all kinds of bad weather, with no complaint about a lobby too small to hold them or a pathetic candy stand. One sees the same faces every weekend. The test of die-hard Fine Arts patrons is whether they know the back way to the theater, bypassing most of Central Avenue's tackiness.
But not all tackiness is created equal. The same aesthetes who shun the multiplexes in Yonkers, where noisy teenagers predominate and the streetscape is solid neon, flock to an unremarkable Carvel stand, one of three on Central Avenue, that is deemed a local treasure.
As legend has it, recounted by Abdol Faghihi, owner of the stand, Tom Carvel lived at this location in 1934, behind the store where the frozen-custard craze was born. Mr. Faghihi's eyes gleam, one immigrant entrepreneur appreciating another. The Carvel empire grew to 700 stands, worth $80 million when the business changed hands in 1989. Mr. Carvel was the first to sell franchises, like the KFC's, Dunkin' Donuts and McDonald's that line Central Avenue. His awkward commercials -- "Buy one, get one free" -- entered the language. 
Mr. Faghihi's stand is flanked by Noodle Kidoodle and Barnes & Noble. When the toy store opened, ice cream sales shot up. When the bookstore added a Starbucks, sales fell. Eventually things returned to normal. At this time of year, a trickle of parents buy birthday cakes during the day, and there is modest after-dinner traffic. Summers are a different matter, with lines from morning until night.
The ebb and flow is a reminder that while fashions on Central Avenue change, some things are forever, like the joy of cruising to Carvel in a cool car.
Witness Richard Cohen of Scarsdale, middle-aged, divorced and a technical supervisor at CBS Sports. In a 1980 MG, he was out buying blinds the other afternoon and couldn't resist a half-and-half cone, that unmistakable swirl of vanilla and chocolate, delicious even on a blustery day.

HEADLINE: SECURITY FORCES IN BELGRADE STORM MILOSEVIC'S HOUSE 

BYLINE:  By CARLOTTA GALL 

DATELINE: BELGRADE, Serbia, Saturday, March 31 

 BODY:
After a night of conflicting reports that Slobodan Milosevic had been arrested on corruption charges, Serbian special forces stormed his residence before dawn today, beginning the legal process that could finally imprison the man whose 13-year reign over Yugoslavia brought wars, destruction and poverty to his people. 
 About 2:30 a.m. local time, several carloads of black-clad special forces commandos drove up to the front gate of the compound where Mr. Milosevic lives, trying to force open the gate and firing several shots, which were met with fire from within the walls. At least one man was wounded. The men succeeded in crashing through the gatehouse, but by 4 a.m. they had not penetrated the walls of the compound. 
 As the attack subsided, police officials met with Milosevic and presented him with an arrest warrant, the Associated Press reported. But Milosevic refused to accept it, saying he did not "recognize these police and these authorities, all of them being NATO servants," an Interior Ministry source told the news agency. 
Shortly after daylight, police pushed all of the Milosevic supporters and journalists about a half-mile down a road away from the villa. More police arrived in the area and blocked off all roads leading to the compound. Some police officers suggested that a final operation would be under way. 
The Bush administration was informed at 5:30 Friday afternoon that Mr. Milosevic, who is wanted on international war crimes charges, was in custody on corruption charges, a senior administration official said tonight.
But as the evening progressed, the administration was receiving reports that he had been released, and officials said they were waiting to hear whether he was still in custody. Even later Friday night, the State Department was questioning whether he had been arrested at all. 
Adding to the confusion, Deputy Prime Minister Zarko Korac said that Mr. Milosevic had been arrested and taken to the Palace of Justice, the central court, sometime before midnight Friday, but that the government would not make an official statement until today. Serbian state television also reported the news, quoting officials.
Television footage showed four black police jeeps drawing up to the Palace of Justice's underground entrance, though the passengers were not visible through the darkened windows, but Mr. Milosevic was not seen leaving his residence, which was surrounded by supporters and journalists from early evening. 
Just a few hours later, Mr. Milosevic appeared at the gates of his house in the prosperous district of Dedinje to greet supporters. "I am not afraid," he told the Belgrade-based independent radio and television outlet B92. "I expect this story to end in a just manner and for the benefit of our people." He did not say whether he had been detained.
"I am just drinking coffee with my comrades and I am very well," he said. "In front of my house many have gathered who have our people and national dignity at heart. We are very proud." 
Then, at 2 a.m., 100 to 150 policemen in full riot gear appeared at the back entrance to the compound, trying to control the crowd and apparently clearing a path. Half an hour later they were followed by a van and cars carrying the special forces units.
Bush administration officials said Mr. Milosevic had not been arrested on charges of crimes against humanity, brought nearly two years ago in connection with war crimes committed by Yugoslav forces in Kosovo. If corruption charges are brought against him, he will be tried locally. 
The Bush administration is deciding this weekend whether to certify Belgrade's compliance with conditions attached to the continuation of United States aid after a deadline of today. One factor the Bush administration will take into account for certification is the level of Belgrade's cooperation with the United Nations international war crimes tribunal at The Hague. This month, Yugoslavia has sent two indictees to The Hague for trial.
Congress appropriated $100 million for Yugoslavia last year. By now, about half has been dispersed. The rest, except for humanitarian aid, depends on certification. Failure to win it would be a blow to the Serbian government and its ailing economy, since the decision would also block financial assistance from the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund.
Early this month, the Bush administration gave Belgrade a list of actions that could aid certification. The first item read: "Arrest Milosevic and transfer him to prison." The administration is not demanding Mr. Milosevic's immediate transfer to The Hague, but his arrest and detention on charges of corruption or abuse of power would probably ensure certification.
Hundreds of Milosevic supporters rushed to take up guard around his house Friday evening, after members of his Socialist Party said that special police units and ambulances had arrived outside his residence to arrest him. 
The head of the Socialist parliamentary faction, Branislav Ivkovic, announced the arrest plans around 7 p.m. Friday during a televised session of the Serbian Parliament. Warning that Mr. Milosevic might be arrested, he called people to go to his house to defend him.
He and other officials arrived at the house, emerging later to tell supporters that Mr. Milosevic was inside and thanked the protesters for rallying to his defense. 
Several black jeeps with darkened windows were parked down the street from the back entrance to the house, a government residence that Mr. Milosevic occupies despite his ouster. Inside the jeeps, men sat without moving. A police prison van and two ambulances were also parked down the street.
Within half an hour of the call to defend Mr. Milosevic, several hundred supporters, most middle-aged or pensioners, were gathered in front of the high gates at the front and back entrances of the house. Some carried batons and handed around a bottle of liquor to ward off the cold. They wore white stickers on their clothes that read, "Slobo, vrati se," or "Slobo, come back."
Every so often they raised the chant "Slobo! Slobo!" and a few shouted and jostled with journalists. As the night wore on, a younger and tougher-looking group joined the crowd ringing the walls of the compound.
The supporters have been guarding the house in shifts for over a month as Mr. Milosevic's arrest has become more likely with Serbian government investigations into his and associates' dealings.
Twice this week, the police tried to deliver a summons for both Mr. Milosevic and his wife, Mirjana Markovic, to submit to questioning, according to a member of the Yugoslav Left, Ms. Markovic's political party. But the guards at the gate refused to accept the summons. 
On Monday the police arrested seven of Mr. Milosevic's allies, in a move expected to produce more evidence against him. He left behind few written records of his 13 years in power, and Serbia's new leaders have struggled to find direct evidence linking him to any crimes.
But the Serbian prime minister, Zoran Djindjic, said the latest detentions were nevertheless an "important step closer" to indicting Mr. Milosevic.
Mr. Korac, the deputy prime minister, said Friday night that Mr. Milosevic would be questioned by an investigative judge today in connection with financial charges. These are likely to include embezzlement of state funds and purchase of a state villa near his official residence in Dedinje at a low price in 1999.
The Serbian government has been investigating Mr. Milosevic and a number of associates for corruption, embezzlement and abuse of power, and has been building a case against him. Investigators say they have gathered evidence on the house deal but investigations into more serious crimes have proceeded slowly.
Branislav Ivkovic, a leader of the pro-Milosevic Socialist Party, went to the federal presidency building, apparently to negotiate with President Vojislav Kostunica or his aides. 
Later, in Parliament, Mr. Ivkovic was the first to announce that an effort was under way to arrest Mr. Milosevic.
Officials in Belgrade said that Mr. Djindjic's government had moved to replace the state security police guard around Mr. Milosevic, and that most had agreed to be replaced.
Mr. Milosevic came to power as the Communist leadership of the country was crumbling. Under his leadership, the country came apart as well as he attempted to put down independence movements in the constituent republics. Yugoslavia lost four wars in nine years: in Slovenia, Croatia, Bosnia and then Kosovo, which was an integral part of Serbia itself.
The war in Bosnia was Europe's worst conflict since World War II; more than 200,000 people died. 
The international indictment charged Mr. Milosevic and other senior officials with crimes including the murder, forced deportation and persecution of Kosovo Albanians.

HEADLINE: The Senate's Moment for Change 

BYLINE:  By RICHARD L. BERKE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 30 

 BODY:
For more than a decade, advocates of overhauling the campaign finance system have been waiting for their moment.
 First, they hoped that the fund-raising scandals involving Charles H. Keating Jr., the Arizona savings and loan executive, would embolden Congress. Then they figured that Ross Perot would goad the political establishment into action. Then they thought that the disclosures about President Bill Clinton offering big donors overnight stays in the Lincoln Bedroom might be the answer. 
 Now, finally, the moment has come.
With the Senate widely expected on Monday to approve comprehensive legislation to prohibit unrestricted donations from being pumped into the political system, many Democrats and Republicans credited Senator John McCain, Republican of Arizona, who made campaign finance overhaul the centerpiece of his presidential campaign last year, as the catalyst for the legislation that bears his name. (His own interest in change was incited by his entanglement in the Keating Five case.) 
Yet beyond Mr. McCain's crusade, many who have long followed campaign finance machinations in Congress argued that many less obvious factors were at play.
One explanation is that the Senate simply changed. About a half-dozen new Democratic senators made it to Washington after pledging to refashion the campaign finance system.
"What changed, in part, was the makeup of the Senate, 50-50," said Senator Tom Daschle of South Dakota, the Democratic leader. "Most of the votes, as you have seen, have come from us."
As an example, in a debate last year in the contest for senator from Michigan, Debbie Stabenow, a Democrat, accused the incumbent Republican, Spencer Abraham, of blocking the legislation sponsored by Mr. McCain and Senator Russell D. Feingold, Democrat of Wisconsin.
"If Senator Abraham was not one of five senators holding it up in the Senate," she said then, "we would not be having this conversation. Campaign finance reform is what we need."
Ms. Stabenow, now a senator, favors the bill. (Mr. Abraham is now secretary of energy.)
Another factor is that with the proliferation of unregulated spending, some lawmakers have come around to believe that there should be new restrictions -- or else the system they so relied upon may damage their own re-election prospects.
It marks a sharp shift given that many incumbents for years did not dare tinker with a system that got them elected in the first place. (That does not mean that lawmakers who support the legislation are comfortable with it; many, in fact, are downright frightened.)
"It's an irony that the system spun so out of control that even the incumbents got afraid of it," said David B. Magleby, a political scientist at Brigham Young University who specializes in campaign finance. "These guys feel a lot more threatened because of the ability of their opponents to target soft money and use issue advocacy attacks on them. These folks crave control of their electoral futures."
An important turning point was the announcement in January by Senator Thad Cochran, a conservative Republican from Mississippi, endorsing the McCain-Feingold legislation. In explaining his move, Mr. Cochran complained that outside groups had swamped candidates by pouring resources into states and Congressional districts.
"Candidates are now becoming defenseless against the huge amounts of money that are now being spent in these campaigns," Mr. Cochran said.
Another explanation that Mr. Daschle did not mention was Mr. Daschle himself. For days he moved behind the scenes to buttonhole Democrats who were threatening to bolt, like Senators Paul S. Sarbanes of Maryland, Tom Harkin of Iowa and Daniel K. Inouye of Hawaii.
"I used some fairly tough rhetoric with regard to my colleagues when I talked about this being the true test for Democrats, as we had to stick together," Mr. Feingold said. "Well, because of Tom Daschle, we did."
Another factor is the growing sense of equilibrium between the parties. For years, Democrats complained that proposals dreamed up by Republicas were tilted to help Republicans -- and vice versa. This time, there is less of a sense that the legislation would unduly damage one party over the other.
For all the self-congratulations and high expectations in the Senate, there are no assurances that the legislation will survive the House. Still, there are high hopes among the bill's proponents. The Senate last passed sweeping campaign finance legislation in 1993. And at that time, there was no expectation that the measure would make it to the president's desk. It did not.
During the internal maneuvering throughout the debate over an overhaul this year, politicians were just as likely to cite such outside threats to their own campaigns as they were to mention any public groundswell. Polls show that while Americans overwhelmingly favor a change, it is well below fixing the economy or improving schools on their list of priorities. Politicians and strategists still debate whether the issue could be a career-killer for a politician who votes against an overhaul.
"If Americans really wanted campaign finance reform, John McCain would be president," said Alex Castellanos, a Republican media consultant who opposes the legislation. "This is not a bottom-up cause."
Mike Murphy, a McCain strategist, countered that the senator could take some, but not all, of the credit. "McCain was the spark plug; he created an environment where the cause of reform got legs outside the do-gooder lobbying in Washington," Mr. Murphy said. "But the closer Senate helped, too."
Indeed, even Bush loyalists are mindful that while Mr. McCain lost the Republican nomination, he had drawn enormous attention from his campaign finance drive -- and that on several recent days he dominated the news more than the president.
"Timing is everything," said David L. Boren, the president of the University of Oklahoma who, as a Democratic senator from Oklahoma, was for years the Senate's biggest crusader for a change. "We were rarely on the front page. It was very frustrating. Now, it's on the front page in part because of the McCain presidential campaign."
Fred Wertheimer, who for years has been the lobbyist most closely associated with pressing to change the system, cited a broader explanation than simply Mr. McCain or the new Senate.
"In our country, we tend to deal with big problems when they become a crisis," Mr. Wertheimer said. "This issue has reached the crisis stage."
Maybe so. But crisis or not, there seems to be consensus on one point: the moment has arrived.

HEADLINE: Journal; 
The Thrill Of It All

BYLINE:  By Frank Rich 

 BODY:
George W. Bush, earnest student of his father's one-term presidency, finally figured out that it is still the economy, stupid, that could do him in. 
 And so on Tuesday Mr. Bush went to Kalamazoo to give what his aides billed as a "major address" in which he stopped saying that the country was on the skids and instead took a sunny view. "The American economy," the president said, "is like a great athlete at the end of the first leg of a long, long race -- somewhat winded, but fundamentally strong." A day later he upped the ante to irrational exuberance, telling a White House conference of high-tech leaders "the future is incredibly bright." 
 If I may mix sports metaphors, Mr. Bush was sucker-punched. After weeks in which the Democrats had pounded him and Dick Cheney for talking down the economy -- "The result of the administration's rhetoric is an economic slowdown," said Tom Daschle -- the president fell into their trap. It's manifestly clear that whatever is happening with the economy, Mr. Bush has had little to do with it. The stock market tech bubble burst last spring, well before he muttered a discouraging word, and it's hard to see how his and Mr. Cheney's poor-mouthing have hurt the economy since. Good signs (low unemployment and inflation, rebounding consumer confidence) continue to mix with the bad. 
Nor does the president's latent Pollyanna-ism make any difference: The day after his televised Kalamazoo cheerleading, the Nasdaq fell almost 6 percent.
The truth is that no one knows for sure where this contradictory economy is going, and it's equally true that almost nothing Mr. Bush says or does (his tax cut most certainly included) will have much of an effect on it. But at a time of uncertainty and dwindling stock portfolios, everyone's looking for a scapegoat. 
The Democrats want to pin the bad news on Mr. Bush -- even if that bad news first hit while Bill Clinton was in the White House. Mr. Bush keeps trying to pin the tail back on Mr. Clinton -- even though he's wasting his breath. If there's an ongoing recession on Mr. Bush's watch during an election cycle, he will be left holding the political bag, just as his father was, no matter who or what is actually responsible. Already this week's ABC News/Washington Post poll shows that only 50 percent of the country approves of his handling of the economy. (Of course, if there is no recession, he'll be upgraded to a genius.)
But there's one major player in this story who clearly isn't blameless, and that's our 24/7 media. Yes, the national hysteria surrounding both the rise and fall of Nasdaq has been fueled, as many have said, by the fact that it's the first time as many as 50 percent of Americans own stock. But if that's part of the story, it's not the whole story. This is also the first time that a major market run-up and collapse have been grist for a mediathon -- our new non-stop multimedia spectacular in which competing cable news services, often abetted by both Web and print journalism, whip a story into a dramatic pitch that is shaped more by the ratings-and-circulation-boosting imperatives of entertainment than by rational news judgment. Back in that ancient, innocent day of 1987 -- the last time the market tanked -- CNN had no competitors, no one had heard of the Web, and there were no financial TV channels examining every quarter-point fluctuation of the Dow. 
Now we have a great news maw that must be fed whether there is news happening or not -- and outfitted with theatrics and filler to sustain the format. In the case of the stock market, James Surowiecki has argued in The New Yorker, the results may have thrown a monkey wrench into the system. "Minute-by-minute coverage" of Wall Street, he wrote, "distorts the way the market works and helps turn what should be a diverse, independent-thinking crowd of investors into a herd acting upon a single collective thought" -- whether to buy or sell. 
This doesn't mean that the tech-bubble mediathon actually drove the economy or market -- any more than it caused Columbine or the JonBenet murder or the Marc Rich pardon or any other story that's gotten wall-to-wall treatment since such epics took root in the mid-1990's. But the overwrought coverage did have a megaphone effect on the highs and lows alike, and at times bordered on the ludicrous. 
Two weeks ago MSNBC brought back its N.B.A. countdown clock to ratchet up suspense for the Fed's announcement of another predictable half-point rate cut. After the Fed's action failed to produce a rally, Alan Greenspan was himself discounted for dramatic effect, his media image transformed almost overnight from the Obi Wan Kenobi of our economy to its doddering King Lear. 
Our mediathons also require the constant minting of new celebrity bloviators who can fill all that air time with spin. In lieu of the criminal lawyers who played that role during the O. J. Simpson trial and the "former prosecutors" and second-rung law school professors of Monicagate came a parade of Wall Street analysts. The stars of the tech bubble included Henry Blodget of Merrill Lynch, who caused a run on Amazon after predicting that the profit-free company would hit $400 a share (it's now around $10), and Mary Meeker of Morgan Stanley, who championed priceline.com and drugstore.com, among other picks that have now fallen as much as 96 percent in value. "I did not aspire to be a media celebrity in any way, shape or form," Ms. Meeker told The Guardian this month as her media star crashed with a thud worthy of William Ginsburg's. No doubt she posed for the cover of Fortune and let a reporter record her market pronouncements for a New Yorker profile at gunpoint. 
"If you go back 10 years, basically an analyst's job was to tell the client what the analyst thought, not to act as a guru," says another major analyst (albeit with a better track record), who has resisted the urge to become a media hound. Once analysts started to appear on TV to promote themselves and their wares, they were deified like any other media stars, with the consequence that amateur investors overvalued their utterances. "To me, CNBC is only a financial soap opera," this analyst adds, "and you know how addictive soap operas are. The cycle fed on itself until the bubble burst." 
No one should be amazed that the media construct these shows. Now that so many news organizations are owned by entertainment companies, it's inevitable that, as CBS's Andy Rooney has put it, "Most of the decisions made in television news are not about news, they're about money." And print has hardly been immune from joining the act, especially during the dot-com boom and crash. 
What is amazing is how much tolerance the public has for our new hyperbolic news format. The only time there's been any serious revolt was a month ago, when the weather was covered much like the bull market -- or for that matter, like the exit polls on our last election night. Brandishing scientific models and the usual array of doppler toys, our infotainers, desperate for drama that might keep us watching, predicted a blizzard that became the meteorological equivalent of an Amazon stock forecast -- variously billed as the biggest in 50, 150, even 300 years. (There have been official weather records in America for only 115 years.) When the apocalypse didn't arrive, people got angry -- perhaps because they had been directly affected this time, by flight, school and work cancellations. It's only when press hysteria befalls other people that it is so irresistibly entertaining.
What mediathon will come next? My bet is on mad cow. There is no reported instance of this disease in the U.S., but already you can see the hype starting as the media cast about for a new drama at a time of slow news. Grim video clips from Europe are being recycled at an accelerating pace, and now foot-and-mouth disease (which poses no threat to humans) is being frequently conflated on TV with mad cow. The seizure of some Vermont sheep for testing last weekend received breathless coverage even though it was virtually news-free; the Department of Agriculture had first put out a public alert about this suspect flock nine months ago. 
An internal beef-industry document this week says that "the biggest threat mad cow poses for the United States so far is in the consumer fear instigated by national media reports." But why should that stop anyone? Entertainment rules, and a beef scare, valid or not, could be even more riveting than the mauling of Jeff Bezos by the bears.
